Gwin v. Motor Vehicle Admin.


No. 91, September Term, 2004


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


385 Md. 440; 869 A.2d 822; 2005 Md. LEXIS 107



March 10, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Gwin v. Md Motor Vehicle Admin., 126 S. Ct. 359, 163 L. Ed. 2d 67, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 6064 (U.S., Oct. 3, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County. Pamela L. North, JUDGE.
Gwin v. MVA, 383 Md. 569, 861 A.2d 60, 2004 Md. LEXIS 741 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certiorari to an appeal by petitioner driver's license applicant from a judgment of the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County (Maryland), reversing an administrative law judge's decision and upholding denial, by respondent Maryland Motor Vehicle Administration (MVA), of a request that the MVA process a driver's license application. The MVA argued the applicant was ineligible under Md. Code Ann., Transp. § 16-103.1(1) (2002).


OVERVIEW: The applicant was under revocation in two states because of past drunk driving offenses, and his revocation in Florida was permanent. The Interstate Driver License Compact, codified in Maryland at Md. Code Ann., Transp. § 16-703 (2002), allowed for a person who moved to a new state while under suspension or revocation to apply, though not necessarily receive, a license in the new state of residence after a one-year waiting period, with the proviso "if permitted by law." That proviso made it possible for the court to harmonize the Compact with Md. Code Ann., Transp. § 16-103.1(1) (2002). There was no point in ordering the MVA to process the application, because § 16-103.1 made the applicant ineligible as a matter of law. Nothing prevented the legislature, in seeking to effectuate the legislative intent of promoting safety, from setting stricter requirements.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

234 of 744 DOCUMENTS


State v. Snowden


No. 42, September Term, 2004


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


385 Md. 64; 867 A.2d 314; 2005 Md. LEXIS 35



February 7, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Montgomery County). Durke G. Thompson, JUDGE.
Snowden v. State, 156 Md. App. 139, 846 A.2d 36, 2004 Md. App. LEXIS 32 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certiorari to the State's appeal from a reversal, by the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland, of defendant's convictions of sexual abuse of three young girls. The intermediate court had held that the admission, pursuant to Md. Code Ann., Crim. Proc. § 11-304 (2001), the tender years statute, of the girls' statements to a social worker violated defendant's confrontation guarantees, as interpreted in Crawford v. Washington.


OVERVIEW: As soon as the police investigation of the girls' allegations got under way, they were interviewed, in accordance with Maryland practice, by a state social worker at a child protective facility. The statements elicited by the social worker were introduced at trial after the trial court determined that the girls were unavailable to testify because of potential trauma. Defense counsel expressed state and federal constitutional objections at that time, and those objections preserved the issue for appeal. While appeal to the intermediate court was pending, the nation's highest court clarified the extent of confrontation rights in the Crawford decision, holding that an unavailable declarant's testimonial statements, that is, statements that a declarant objectively would know were likely to be used in a prosecution, could not be used at trial unless there had been a prior opportunity to cross-examine the declarant. Although the girls were young, there was no reason to think that they were not well aware that the statements were being taken because of the police investigation their accusations had launched, so the statements were testimonial, not therapeutic, in their basic nature.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the intermediate court.

 

235 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Comptroller of the Treasury v. Phillips


No. 46, September Term, 2004


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


384 Md. 583; 865 A.2d 590; 2005 Md. LEXIS 6



January 13, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Talbot County). William S. Horne, JUDGE.
Comptroller v. Phillips, 381 Md. 677, 851 A.2d 596, 2004 Md. LEXIS 400 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certiorari on its own initiative before the Maryland Court of Special Appeals could consider appellant Comptroller of the Maryland Treasury's challenge to a decision of the Circuit Court for Talbot County (Maryland), affirming an administrative decision that appellee decedent's estate was not liable for Maryland estate tax because no federal estate tax had been due.


OVERVIEW: Md. Code Ann., Tax-Gen. § 7-304 (1997 & 2001 Cum. Supp.), the version of Maryland's pick-up estate tax statute applicable to the estate, provided that taxes were due to the extent to which the federal credit for state death taxes exceeded Maryland inheritance taxes. In the normal situation, an estate took advantage of the unified credit against federal estate tax and the calculation was made. In the instant case, no estate tax was due because of another federal estate tax credit, the prior transfer credit, which was available because two successive transfers occurred when the decedents died within a few days of each other. The Comptroller argued that Maryland estate tax was due because the wrong sort of credit had been taken, even though the estate had not owed any estate tax once the prior transfer credit was applied. Because § 7-304(c) was ambiguous, the court looked to the statute's legislative history and to prior case law. It was clear that the subsection was intended to prevent late filers of federal estate tax from claiming that no Maryland estate tax was due because no federal tax was paid. It was a different matter when some other credit meant that no federal tax was due.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision.

Lee v. Cline

COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND

DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted plaintiff driver's petition for a writ of certiorari challenging the judgment of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which affirmed a trial court judgment in favor of defendants, a county, a sheriff department, its sheriff, a deputy sheriff, and others. The trial court had granted summary judgment in favor of defendants upon determining that the deputy sheriff had qualified immunity under the Maryland Tort Claims Act, Md. The court found error and held that public official immunity was inapplicable in actions against public officials for violations of a plaintiff's rights guaranteed by the Maryland Constitution. The court did clarify that the deputy sheriff, as an individual, was immune for damages and was substituted by the State.

County Comm'rs v. Carroll Craft Retail, Inc.


No. 21, September Term, 2004


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


384 Md. 23; 862 A.2d 404; 2004 Md. LEXIS 775



December 3, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended December 7, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] In the Circuit Court for Carroll County. Case No. C-2003-38589. Appeal from the Circuit Court for Carroll County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Raymond E. Bach, Sr., JUDGE.
Carroll County v. Craft, 381 Md. 324, 849 A.2d 473, 2004 Md. LEXIS 264 (2004)



DISPOSITION: WRIT OF CERTIORARI DISMISSED, WITH COSTS, AS HAVING BEEN IMPROVIDENTLY GRANTED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Writ of certiorari was granted, prior to a definitive ruling by the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland, to examine whether county zoning ordinances dealing with adult entertainment businesses were unconstitutionally vague and ambiguous.


OVERVIEW: After a county sought to enjoin the operation of a retail store for violating county zoning ordinances regarding the operation of adult entertainment businesses, the owners of the building where the store was located and the operator of the store challenged the constitutionality of the zoning ordinances. Proceedings and appeals were conducted in district and circuit courts. After granting a writ of certiorari on its own initiative, the court of appeals found that the county previously had no right to appeal a judgment entered in a district court appeal to the court of special appeals. What the county should have done was to file a petition for certiorari with the court of appeals. Therefore, the court of special appeals should have denied the motion and immediately transferred the case to the court of appeals. Further, if the county desired to challenge a circuit court's order, it was required to note an appeal. When it failed to do so within the 30 days allowed, the order became final; and, the appeal was dismissed. Thus, no appeal was properly before the court of appeals.


OUTCOME: The writ of certiorari was dismissed.

Suessmann v. Lamone


No. 140, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


383 Md. 697; 862 A.2d 1; 2004 Md. LEXIS 734



November 17, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for St. Mary's County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Case No. 18-C-04-000218 IJ. Richard H. Sothoron; Warren J. Krug; Christopher C. Henderson, JUDGES.
Suessmann v. Lamone, 380 Md. 436, 845 A.2d 579, 2004 Md. LEXIS 171 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Court provided reasons for prior order and addressed reserved issues.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant voters sought review of the order from the Circuit Court for St. Mary's County (Maryland), which denied their petition for declaratory and injunctive relief seeking to enjoin certification of the results of the primary election for judicial offices and an order directing appellee county boards of elections to conduct new primary elections for judicial offices in which all registered voters in the counties could participate.


OVERVIEW: The voters alleged that their rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and the Maryland Declaration of Rights were violated because state election law permitted the exclusion of unaffiliated voters like themselves from participating in judicial primary elections. The circuit court denied their petition for relief. On appeal, the court affirmed, first finding that the voters' complaint should have been dismissed because it did not satisfy the Md. Code Ann., Elec. § 12-202(a)(2), which required a party challenging an election to prove by clear and convincing evidence that there was a substantial probability that the outcome of the election would have been changed had it not been for the contested procedure. The court then found that the State had a legitimate interest in a policy of keeping partisanship out of judicial elections as far as possible without abandoning the long-established infrastructure of political party primaries. Its attempts to achieve that goal did not violate the equal protection provisions of either the Maryland or federal constitutions simply because some voters who declined to join a political party nevertheless wished to vote in that party's primary.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial.

Davis v. Slater


No. 26, September Term 2004


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


383 Md. 599; 861 A.2d 78; 2004 Md. LEXIS 723



November 15, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court Baltimore County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. J. Norris Byrnes, JUDGE.
Davis v. Slater, 381 Md. 673, 851 A.2d 593, 2004 Md. LEXIS 329 (2004)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner injured person appealed from a judgment of the Baltimore County Circuit Court (Maryland) in favor of respondent driver. The injured person had brought a civil suit requesting $ 25,000 in damages as a result of a vehicular collision between the parties. The trial court denied the injured person's motion to strike the driver's jury demand after the injured person reduced his ad damnum clause from $ 25,000 to $ 10,000.


OVERVIEW: After the injured person filed his complaint in a Maryland District Court, the driver elected a jury trial pursuant to Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 4-402(e)(1) (1999), and the case was transferred to the trial court. The parties engaged in discovery for 18 months, and the injured person subsequently filed an amended complaint reducing his demand for damages to $ 10,000. The injured person then filed his motion to strike the jury demand made by the driver, which was denied. The injured person asserted that the trial court erred as a matter of law when it held that the driver was still entitled to a jury trial despite the fact that the amount in controversy no longer exceeded $ 10,000 because access to a jury trial was triggered solely by the amount in controversy, and his reduction of the amount in controversy divested the driver of her entitlement to a jury trial. The court disagreed and, upon finding that the Maryland General Assembly had never abrogated the common law entitlement to a jury, held that § 4-402(e)(1) did not abrogate the common law entitlement to a jury trial when the amount in controversy failed to exceed $ 10,000.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

240 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Davis v. State


No. 59/36, September Term, 2002


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


383 Md. 394; 859 A.2d 1112; 2004 Md. LEXIS 711



October 21, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Baltimore City), William Quarles, JUDGE.
Davis v. State, 144 Md. App. 144, 797 A.2d 84, 2002 Md. App. LEXIS 75 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants moved, pre-trial, to suppress evidence seized during a search executed pursuant to a warrant. The motion was denied. Defendants were convicted of possession of marijuana with intent to distribute and a handgun violation. The Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) affirmed the judgments of the circuit court. Defendants' petitions for certiorari were granted.


OVERVIEW: The evidence defendants sought to suppress was obtained when officers executed a warrant implicitly authorizing a "no-knock" entry. The issue before the court was whether, where there was no statute so providing, a judge was authorized to issue a "no-knock" warrant, on the basis of which the police could made a "no-knock" entry to execute the search and seizure warrant. The supreme court rejected the rule implicitly, if not expressly, enunciated by the appellate court in various opinions, requiring applicants for search warrants to obtain pre-entry authorization for a "no-knock" entry in addition to the authorization for the search. The supreme court held that a judicial officer in Maryland could not issue a "no-knock" warrant. Rather, the propriety of a "no-knock" entry would be reviewed and determined on the basis of the facts known to the officers at the time of entry, rather than at the time of the application for the warrant. The supreme court further found that the entry in the instant case was not justified by existing and articulated exigency.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of special appeals was reversed. The case was remanded with instructions to vacate the judgment of the circuit court and remand to that court for further proceedings.

Baker v. State


No. 14, September Term, 2004


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


383 Md. 550; 861 A.2d 48; 2004 Md. LEXIS 612



October 8, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration granted by, Claim disallowed by Baker v. State, 383 Md. 550, 861 A.2d 48, 2004 Md. LEXIS 721 (2004)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Baker v. Maryland, 544 U.S. 1001, 125 S. Ct. 1931, 161 L. Ed. 2d 775, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 3569 (2005)
Post-conviction relief denied at Baker v. State, 389 Md. 127, 883 A.2d 916, 2005 Md. LEXIS 586 (2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Motion to Correct Illegal Sentence (Death Penalty - Circuit Court for Harford County), Emory A. Plitt, Jr., JUDGE.
Baker v. State, 377 Md. 567, 833 A.2d 1070, 2003 Md. LEXIS 706 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After defendant's murder conviction and death sentence were affirmed, he moved the Circuit Court for Harford County (Maryland) to correct an illegal sentence and for new sentencing. The trial court denied the motion, and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant said Ring v. Arizona rendered Maryland's death penalty statute unconstitutional because it used a preponderance of the evidence standard to weigh aggravating factors against mitigating factors, when imposing the death penalty. The court of appeals held this was not a proper issue in defendant's case because the trial court did not find any mitigating factors existed, when it sentenced him, after he waived sentencing by a jury. A weighing of factors played no role in his sentencing. Even if such a weighing had occurred, Maryland's preponderance of the evidence standard in weighing aggravating circumstances against mitigating circumstances was not unconstitutional and did not invalidate Maryland's capital punishment law. It had been held in defendant's prior appeal that he made a knowing and voluntary waiver of his right to jury sentencing. He was properly advised, at that time, of the burden of proof to establish mitigating factors. Under Md. R. 4-343, once the sentencing authority found that one or more aggravating circumstances were proved beyond a reasonable doubt and no mitigating circumstances existed, the sentence was required to be death.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

Levitt v. Fax.com, Inc.


No. 21, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


383 Md. 141; 857 A.2d 1089; 2004 Md. LEXIS 593



September 13, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Marcella A. Holland, JUDGE.
Levitt v. Fax.com, 374 Md. 582, 824 A.2d 58, 2003 Md. LEXIS 344 (2003)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney challenged the judgment of the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which granted a renewed motion for summary judgment filed by appellee advertisers and which dismissed the case. The attorney, who was granted class action status, brought suit against the advertisers asserting a violation of the Telephone Consumer Protection Act (Act), 47 U.S.C.S. § 227(b)(3), and sought statutory damages.


OVERVIEW: The cause of action arose from the attorney receiving unwanted facsimile advertisements. The advertisers engaged in the business of sending mass advertisements by facsimile. The trial court had granted the advertisers' renewed motion for summary judgment after an appellate court case opinion, which held that the trial courts of Maryland were not to entertain any federal private cause of action created by the Act. The only new issue in the case at bar was the advertisers' argument that the U.S. Congress's creation of a private cause of action, with exclusive jurisdiction in state courts and without a state right to opt out of such jurisdiction, exceeded Congress's authority under U.S. Const. art. I, § 8. The court disagreed and reversed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that the Act, to the extent that it conferred upon appropriate state courts exclusive required jurisdiction to entertain a federal cause of action, did not violate U.S. Const. art. I or U.S. Const. amend. X, based on the mostly concurrent state court jurisdiction that existed over federal claims.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

243 of 744 DOCUMENTS


State v. Raines


No. 129, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


383 Md. 1; 857 A.2d 19; 2004 Md. LEXIS 504



August 26, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. S. Michael Pincus, JUDGE.
State v. Raines, 382 Md. 58, 853 A.2d 784, 2004 Md. LEXIS 451 (Md., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certiorari review to the State's interlocutory appeal from a Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland) grant of defendant's motion to suppress DNA evidence being used to prosecute him for rape, on grounds that the Maryland DNA Collection Act, Md. Code Ann., Pub. Safety § 2-501 et seq. (2003), violated U.S. Const. amend. IV. Defendant appealed the trial court's determination that the Act was not an ex post facto law.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was incarcerated for another offense when, like all inmates, he was subject to taking of a DNA by a swab of his mouth for data collection purposes. The sample eventually was used to link him to an unsolved rape, at which point he challenged the constitutionality of the Act. The high court held that the intrusion was not an unreasonable one for Fourth Amendment purposes. It was far less intrusive than a blood test and amply justified by the interest in solving crime. Furthermore, as an inmate, defendant's expectation of privacy was greatly diminished. The trial court correctly found, however, that the Act was not an ex post facto law under either the U.S. or Maryland constitutions, because, like sex offender registration schemes, it was regulatory rather than punitive. If it was not a criminal law, it could not be ex post facto.


OUTCOME: The court agreed with the trial court's holding as to the ex post facto clause, but disagreed with it regarding search and seizure law. Therefore, it reversed the order of suppression and remanded.

Evans v. Wilson


No. 123, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


382 Md. 614; 856 A.2d 679; 2004 Md. LEXIS 502



August 24, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Marcella A. Holland JUDGE.
Evans v. Wilson, 379 Md. 225, 841 A.2d 339, 2004 Md. LEXIS 67 (2004)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certiorari review to an appeal by plaintiff purported father from an order of the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland) that dismissed his complaint seeking orders of visitation and to determine paternity of a child born to defendant mother while she was married to and living with another man. He argued that the trial court was required to order genetic testing by Md. Code Ann., Fam. Law § 5-1029 (1999).


OVERVIEW: The mother dated the purported father before she married her husband, but admitted to having had only one sexual encounter with the purported father thereafter. She admitted having led him to believe he was the child's father, but called it a sick game, engaged in because of feelings of guilt about an abortion. The high court agreed that genetic testing was not mandated under the circumstances and that the trial court had not abused its discretion in weighing the best interests of the child and determining that preservation of a stable family unit was paramount in the case of the child in question. Because of the presumption of legitimacy of Md. Code Ann., Est. & Trusts § 1-206 (2001), the purported father lacked the status of a putative father within the meaning of Md. Code Ann., Fam. Law § 5-1002(c) (1999), which was a prerequisite to being able to demand blood or genetic testing under Md. Code Ann., Fam. Law § 5-1029 (1999). That law designed to enable men to avoid being falsely identified as fathers of children born out of wedlock. Finally, the purported father failed to identify any cognizable constitutional violation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Queen Anne's Conservation, Inc. v. County Comm'rs


No. 108, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


382 Md. 306; 855 A.2d 325; 2004 Md. LEXIS 464



July 29, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Foley v. Hovnanian, 399 Md. 595, 925 A.2d 634, 2007 Md. LEXIS 367 (2007)
Related proceeding at Md. Bd. of Pub. Works v. K. Hovnanian's Four Seasons at Kent Island, LLC, 2012 Md. LEXIS 206 (Md., Apr. 23, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Queen Anne's County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. John W. Sause, Jr., JUDGE.
Queen Anne's Conservation v. County Comm'rs, 379 Md. 224, 841 A.2d 339, 2004 Md. LEXIS 57 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs association and citizens sued defendants developer and county commissioners in the Circuit Court for Queen Anne's County (Maryland) to declare the commissioners' development rights and responsibilities agreement with the developer invalid. The trial court dismissed the case, and plaintiffs appealed. Before the special appeals court could consider the matter, the court of appeals sua sponte issued a writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs objected to a development rights and responsibilities agreement (DRRA) between the county commissioners and the developer. The court of appeals held their proper recourse was an administrative appeal to the county's board of appeals, under Md. Ann. Code art. 66B, § 4.07 (2003), which was to hear and decide appeals alleging an administrative officer's error in enforcing art. 66B, and, if aggrieved by the Board's final action, a petition for judicial review. The county commissioners acted legislatively when authorizing DRRAs in Queen Anne's County, Md., and reserving to themselves the role of "public principal" authorized to enter into such agreements. When they signed off on a DRRA with the developer, this was done in their administrative role as the "public principal." Their approval was the act of an "administrative officer" or "administrative official" under the Maryland and County Codes, respectively, because the term "administrative official" embraced whatever administrative mechanism a local Maryland jurisdiction set up to enforce its planning and zoning laws and ordinances, including a multi-member body.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

Md. Military Dep't v. Cherry


No. 98, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


382 Md. 117; 854 A.2d 1200; 2004 Md. LEXIS 459; 150 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P34,890



July 27, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County. Case No. C-2002-77099.
Md. Military v. Cherry, 378 Md. 617, 837 A.2d 928, 2003 Md. LEXIS 920 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, current or former state-employed airport firefighters of the Maryland Military Department, sought review from a judgment of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland). The judgment vacated the circuit court's grant of the State's motion to dismiss their action, wherein the firefighters sought overtime wages for attending National Guard drills, annual training, and other federally mandated military training.


OVERVIEW: The firefighters were required to attend various training sessions as part of their membership in the National Guard, which membership was a condition of their employment. The only compensation received for the National Guard service was pursuant to 37 U.S.C.S. §§ 204(a)(2) and 206(a). The firefighters filed suit in the circuit court, seeking overtime wages from the State pursuant to a variety of federal and state statutes. On further appeal, the court found that the firefighters had failed to exhaust their administrative remedies before seeking review of a final administrative decision in circuit court, such that there was a lack of jurisdiction. The court did not reach the issue as to coverage under the federal law. The court noted that its earlier decision in Bunch was controlling, and that the Kram precedent did not change the outcome.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and remanded the matter to that court with instructions to affirm the circuit court's judgment of dismissal.

Evans v. State


No. 78, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


382 Md. 248; 855 A.2d 291; 2004 Md. LEXIS 453



July 23, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Evans v. Maryland, 543 U.S. 1150, 125 S. Ct. 1325, 161 L. Ed. 2d 113, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 1524 (U.S., 2005)
Subsequent appeal at Evans v. State, 389 Md. 456, 886 A.2d 562, 2005 Md. LEXIS 649 (Md., Nov. 10, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: (DEATH PENALTY) MOTIONS FOR NEW TRIAL AND TO CORRECT ILLEGAL SENTENCE. Christian M. Kahl, JUDGE.
Evans v. State, 333 Md. 660, 637 A.2d 117, 1994 Md. LEXIS 30 (1994)



DISPOSITION: ORDERS OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE COUNTY AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder for killing two people, and was under two death sentences. Defendant filed in the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland) a motion for a new trial based on allegedly newly discovered evidence, pursuant to Md. R. 4-331(c), and a separate motion to correct an illegal sentence, pursuant to Md. R. 4-345(a). The circuit court denied the motions. Defendant timely filed notices of appeal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant confessed to killing two people in a contract killing. He argued that he was entitled to a new sentencing hearing as newly discovered evidence in FBI reports taken at the time showed that he was not the shooter and therefore not eligible for the death penalty. He also argued that the trial judge's application, at his sentencing hearing, of an amendment to the Maryland death penalty statute regarding mitigating circumstances which became effective a few months after the murders violated the Ex Post Facto Clauses of the United States and Maryland Constitutions. Defendant's attorneys argued that newly discovered descriptions in the FBI reports of a man in the lobby where the victims were murdered contradicted the identification of defendant by two witnesses and showed that an African-American male in the lobby was someone other than defendant. On appeal, the court found that a new trial was properly denied, as the report statements did not contradict the two witnesses' testimony, and the evidence was overwhelming that defendant was the triggerman. Further, the application of the amendment to the sentencing hearing did not violate the ex post facto prohibition.


OUTCOME: The orders denying the motions were affirmed.

 

248 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Triggs v. State


No. 118, September Term 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


382 Md. 27; 852 A.2d 114; 2004 Md. LEXIS 315



June 16, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Montgomery County). Ann S. Harrington, JUDGE.
Triggs v. State, 379 Md. 225, 841 A.2d 340, 2004 Md. LEXIS 74 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland that, while it vacated certain other sentences, affirmed a trial court's imposition of 18 consecutive sentences on him pursuant to Md. Code Ann., Fam. Law § 4-509 (1999 version), for 18 threatening phone calls made to his ex-wife in violation of a no-contact protective order.


OVERVIEW: After many, many instances of abuse by defendant, her ex-husband, including threats to kill her and the children, an ex-wife obtained a no-contact protective order. Before it went into effect, defendant took the children on vacation and over several days made repeated phone calls threatening violence. He argued on appeal that because he had made many calls one after the other, they constituted a single or a few transactions, not 18 separate offenses. The high court applied traditional principles of statutory construction to determine the appropriate unit of prosecution for violation of a protective order, and found a clear legislative intent, based on both plain language and legislative history, that each unlawful phone call was a separate violation of the order. Any other interpretation would have removed any incentive to desist after having begun to violate an order. Since 18 separate violations had been established, there was no impropriety in the trial court's decision, in hopes of protecting defendant's family, to impose 18 consecutive sentences.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the multiple sentences.

 

249 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Arroyo v. Bd. of Educ.


No. 114, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


381 Md. 646; 851 A.2d 576; 2004 Md. LEXIS 309; 21 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 779



June 10, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Howard Country pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Lenore Gelfman, JUDGE.
Arroyo v. Bd. of Educ., 379 Md. 224, 841 A.2d 339, 2004 Md. LEXIS 63 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the termination of petitioner counselor's employment was judicially affirmed, he sued respondents, a county school board and the county, in the Circuit Court for Howard County (Maryland) for wrongful termination. After the county was dismissed from the suit, the trial court granted the board's summary judgment motion. Prior to consideration by the Maryland Court of Special Appeals, the Maryland Court of Appeals issued a writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The counselor said the limitations period applicable to his wrongful termination suit began to run after an appellate court affirmed the administrative decision terminating his employment. The court of appeals held the counselor had to file his wrongful termination suit no later than three years after the final administrative decision terminating his employment was made. This was when his employment was finally terminated. This final administrative decision was left to the discretion of the state board of education, which had primary jurisdiction. This decision exhausted his administrative remedies, as there was nothing left for the state board to do, and it was from at least this point that the statute of limitations applicable to his wrongful termination suit began to run. The subsequent judicial review of the final administrative decision under Md. Code Ann., State Gov't § 10-222 in the trial and appellate courts was not considered part of the counselor's administrative remedies. As the counselor's wrongful termination action was not filed within three years of the decision of the state board of education, the suit was barred by the statute of limitations.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

Worton Creek Marina, LLC v. Claggett


No. 90, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


381 Md. 499; 850 A.2d 1169; 2004 Md. LEXIS 300



June 7, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Circuit Court for Kent County. Civil No. 14-C-01-5025, Robert L. Karwacki, JUDGE.
County Comm'rs v. Claggett, 152 Md. App. 70, 831 A.2d 77, 2003 Md. App. LEXIS 97 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner marina owners and county commissioners were granted certiorari to consider a decision by the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) that the Kent County, Md., Code of Public Local Laws, art. 68, as amended by Kent County, Md., Code of Public Local Laws, Bill No. 7-2001 (county ordinance) was preempted by state statutes and regulations that protected respondent landowner's riparian right to use his shoreline for blind hunting sites.


OVERVIEW: The landowner and the marina owners were in a dispute regarding whether the marina was allowed to moor boats in the landowner's extended property line area, which the landowner planned to use as licensed offshore blind sites for hunting waterfowl. The landowner ultimately filed a suit for a declaration that the county ordinance was invalid since it purported to allow mooring in the landowner's riparian areas until a designated date that extended two months into the wild waterfowl hunting season. The lower court declared the ordinance unconstitutional and a petition for certiorari review was filed. The court granted review and found that the ordinance was preempted by state law. The ordinance exceeded the authority delegated to the county commissioners because they passed a regulation that conflicted with state law. The ordinance conflicted with Md. Code Ann., Nat. Res. § 10-607(d), (g), which allowed the landowner to maintain and license an offshore blind site and by allowing boats to remain moored within the landowner's extended property line area, without his permission, during part of the wild waterfowl season.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision in favor of the landowner and held that the ordinance was void to the extent it permitted boat moorings to remain in the extended property line of a neighboring riparian property owner for any part of wild waterfowl hunting season without the neighboring riparian property owner's permission.

Stearman v. State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co.


No. 67, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


381 Md. 436; 849 A.2d 539; 2004 Md. LEXIS 256



May 14, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Robert E. Cadigan, JUDGE.
Stearman v. State Farm, 377 Md. 111, 832 A.2d 204, 2003 Md. LEXIS 631 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff husband and wife sued defendant insurer; the wife alleged that her husband's negligence caused her injuries, and both plaintiffs sought a declaration that the household exclusion in the insurer's auto liability insurance policy was invalid. The Circuit Court for Baltimore County, Maryland, granted the insurer's motion for summary judgment, declaring that the household exclusion was valid. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: The wife was seriously injured in a one-vehicle accident that occurred while she was a passenger in a vehicle driven by her husband. The policy provided bodily injury liability coverage of $ 100,000 per person, unless the injured party was the insured or a member of the insured's family, in which case the statutory minimum coverage, $ 20,000, applied. Plaintiffs argued that this provision was void as against public policy. The supreme court disagreed. The Legislature prohibited liability coverage of less than the minimum amounts required by Md. Code Ann., Transp. § 17-103(b)(1). A "household exclusion" violated public policy only to the extent that it operated to prevent this mandatory minimum coverage. If the Legislature intended to require complete insurance coverage for injury spouses, it would have said so or increased the mandatory minimum liability limits. The supreme court, under the doctrine of separation of powers, could not invade the province of the Legislature and rewrite the law.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Christopher v. Montgomery County HHS


No. 103, September Term 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


381 Md. 188; 849 A.2d 46; 2004 Md. LEXIS 249



May 12, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. DeLawrence Beard, JUDGE
Christopher v. Dep't of Health, 379 Md. 98, 839 A.2d 741, 2004 Md. LEXIS 4 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court issued, on its own initiative, a writ of certiorari permitting petitioner claimant to appeal the decision of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which affirmed a decision of an Administrative Law Judge (ALJ) that determined that the claimant was not disabled for the purposes of receiving the uncapped excess shelter cost deduction and, therefore, was not entitled to food stamp benefits.


OVERVIEW: The claimant had been terminated from a job for "reasons of disability" but never sought disability benefits and did not consider herself disabled. Because the claimant was not receiving any of the disability benefits required by the definition of disabled found in Md. Regs. Code tit. 07, § 03.17.02B(6), the ALJ concluded that the claimant was not entitled to the uncapped shelter cost deduction provided for in the Food Stamp Act, 7 U.S.C.S. § 2014(e)(6)(A). The claimant argued on appeal that the decision denying her the uncapped shelter deduction was arbitrary and capricious, that she should have been deemed constructively disabled with $ 0 income, and that denial of the uncapped deduction violated her equal protection rights. The court disagreed and held that a food stamp recipient must receive disability benefits in order to become entitled to the uncapped excess shelter cost deduction. The court concluded that "receives" meant "actual receipt" and that, as a result, the ALJ did not abuse her discretion when she found that the claimant was not disabled. The court did not address the constitutional issue raised after disposing of the case on a non-constitutional ground.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision.

Fenner v. State


No. 88, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


381 Md. 1; 846 A.2d 1020; 2004 Md. LEXIS 184



April 12, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Fenner v. Md., 543 U.S. 885, 125 S. Ct. 158, 160 L. Ed. 2d 143, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 5121 (U.S., Oct. 4, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Frederick County). Case #K-01-28360. G. Edward Dwyer, Jr., JUDGE.
Fenner v. State, 378 Md. 613, 837 A.2d 925, 2003 Md. LEXIS 839 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which affirmed a trial court's rulings, denying defendant's motion to suppress inculpatory statements made during a bail hearing where he was not represented by counsel and, ultimately, convicting him of distribution of cocaine and conspiracy to distribute cocaine. Defendant filed a petition for writ of certiorari to the court, which was granted.


OVERVIEW: During trial, defendant moved to suppress statements he made during a bail review hearing where he was unrepresented by counsel, and his motion was denied. Affirming, the court held that, in the context of defendant's bail review hearing, the requirements of Miranda did not apply because, although he was in custody at the time, he was not "interrogated" where the district judge's question, "Is there anything you'd like to tell me about yourself, sir," was merely giving defendant a chance to explain any circumstances bearing on his bail and was not designed to elicit an incriminating statement. Moreover, because defendant's bail review hearing was not a "critical stage" of the pretrial proceedings, i.e., it was not an adversary judicial criminal proceeding, his Sixth Amendment right to provided counsel had not yet attached. And, finally, the court held that the redacted version of the inculpatory statement, as it was read to the jury, was not so vague and misleading as to have made its admittance a clear abuse of discretion by the trial court. It was unquestionably relevant and its probative value was not substantially outweighed by the danger of unfair prejudice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court.

Stern v. Bd. of Regents


No. 85, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


380 Md. 691; 846 A.2d 996; 2004 Md. LEXIS 183



April 12, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Case #C-2003-86236 AA. Stewart Berger, JUDGE.
Stern v. Bd. of Regents, 378 Md. 613, 837 A.2d 925, 2003 Md. LEXIS 844 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant students challenged the decision of the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which held that their contract claim against appellees, the Board of Regents, University System of Maryland and its officials, which challenged a mid-year tuition increase, was barred by the doctrine of sovereign immunity. The Board cross-appealed and contended, inter alia, that it was also immune from the student's action for declaratory relief.


OVERVIEW: Responding to the Legislature's mid-fiscal year budget cuts, the Board authorized its constituent institutions to increase their tuition mid-year. Affirming in part and reversing in part, the court held that the Board was entitled to sovereign immunity and, thus, the students' claims were barred. The court held that (1) Md. Code Ann., Educ. §12-104(b)(3) (2001), which authorized the Board to "sue and be sued," did not waive sovereign immunity because the Legislature did not authorize a method of appropriating the funds for an adverse judgment nor did the Board have the necessary taxing authority; (2) the trial court correctly found that, pursuant to Md. Code Ann., State Gov't §12-201(a) (1999), the Board did not waive sovereign immunity because the alleged contract with the students, namely, the tuition bill, was not signed by a duly authorized person; but (3) the trial court improperly found that sovereign immunity was waived for all actions in which declaratory and/or injunctive relief was sought, and, unlike the declaratory actions where the court found immunity was waived, the students were not challenging the legality of a statute or an official's implementation of a statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the part of the circuit court's order that held that the Board was entitled to sovereign immunity for the students' breach of contract claim, but it reversed the part of the circuit court's order that held that sovereign immunity was waived for declaratory and injunction relief.

Finucan v. Md. Bd. of Physician Quality Assur.


No. 71, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


380 Md. 577; 846 A.2d 377; 2004 Md. LEXIS 174



April 5, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Finucan v. Md Bd. of Quality Assurance, 160 L. Ed. 2d 103, 125 S. Ct. 227, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 6463 (U.S., Oct. 4, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Cecil County). Case # 07-C-01-000023. William S. Horne, JUDGE.
Finucan v. Md. State Bd. of Physician Quality Assur., 151 Md. App. 399, 827 A.2d 176, 2003 Md. App. LEXIS 72 (2003)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner physician appealed from a judgment of the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland that affirmed a trial court's judgment upholding the revocation, by respondent Maryland Board of Physician Quality Assurance, of his license to practice, on grounds that his sexual involvement with a series of patients violated Md. Code Ann., Health Occ. § 14-404 (2003 Supp.).


OVERVIEW: The physician commenced love affairs with a series of women while they were his patients, and continued treating them during their involvements, all of which ended badly for the women. He argued that the sexual involvements were separate from his treatment of them as patients, so that he could not be said to have violated § 14-404. The high court disagreed, as had all tribunals that had reviewed the matter. First, the statute was not unconstitutionally vague as applied to the physician, as it would have been clear to any doctor that a sexual involvement with a patient was forbidden. A physician's duty was to put his patients ahead of his own needs, and the physician in question had repeatedly let his own needs dictate his conduct toward his patients, including, in at least one case, the husband of one of the women. Therefore, Maryland joined a number of other states in holding as a matter of law that a sexual relationship with a patient was an ethical violation warranting license revocation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

256 of 744 DOCUMENTS


State v. Brookins


No. 19, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


380 Md. 345; 844 A.2d 1162; 2004 Md. LEXIS 120



March 16, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected March 18, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Prince George's County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Richard H. Sothoron, JUDGE.
State v. Brookins, 376 Md. 697, 831 A.2d 453, 2003 Md. LEXIS 531 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted direct certiorari review to an appeal by the State from an order of the Circuit Court for Prince George's County (Maryland) that dismissed charges against defendants for allegedly violating Md. Ann. Code art. 33, § 13-209 (2002), recodified as Md. Code Ann., Elec. Law § 13-245 (2003), by paying third persons for performing walk around services on election day, holding that the statute violated free speech guarantees.


OVERVIEW: Md. Ann. Code art. 33, § 13-209 (2002), originally enacted to prevent election corruption, prohibited candidates or others from paying persons for performing what were known as walk around services (such as distributing sample ballots) on election days. Defendants were arrested after engaging homeless people and others to perform such services in a statewide election. They argued that the statute impermissibly limited their federal and state free speech guarantees. The high court noted that constitutional case law was generally highly protective of political speech, subjecting any limitations thereon to strict scrutiny except in cases involving campaign contributions. Section 13-209 was not contribution-related; on the contrary, it limited candidates' abilities to get their message out to voters through one-on-one conversations. Furthermore, many other Maryland statutes attacked the problem of election corruption in more direct and effective ways. Therefore, the statute's limitations on free speech were impermissible.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

257 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Spencer v. Md. State Bd. of Pharm.


No. 36, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


380 Md. 515; 846 A.2d 341; 2004 Md. LEXIS 116



March 11, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Baltimore City). Case No. 24-C-01-001405. Alfred Nance, JUDGE.
Md. State Bd. of Pharm. v. Spencer, 150 Md. App. 138, 819 A.2d 383, 2003 Md. App. LEXIS 26 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner pharmacist appealed respondent pharmacy board's findings that she practiced without a license. The trial court vacated and reversed the board's decision, and the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) reversed and remanded the case to the trial court to remand it to the board, directing the board to delegate the authority to conduct further hearings to the Maryland Office of Administrative Hearings. The pharmacist sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The board denied the pharmacist's request to refer her case to the Office of Administrative Hearings (OAH) as two board members participated in unsuccessful settlement negotiations with her. The court of appeals held the board's decision not to refer the case to the OAH, under Md. Code Ann., State Gov't § 10-205(b), was within its discretion, under Md. Code Ann., State Gov't § 10-222(h)(3)(vi), subject to judicial review under the arbitrary and capricious standard. The Court of Special Appeals exceeded its authority by compelling the board to exercise this discretion. Whether the board erred in not recusing members participating in settlement negotiations was separate from its failure to refer the case to the OAH. Even if the board had to recuse panel members, it was not bound to refer the case to the OAH. Its refusal to refer the case to the OAH was not arbitrary or capricious. Even conceding the error of its failure to recuse certain panel members, that did not make its decision not to refer to the OAH arbitrary or capricious. Double jeopardy did not preclude the board's rehearing, nor did res judicata apply, as there was no final judgment or subsequent cause of action.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was reversed as to its requirement that the board, on remand, refer the matter to the Office of Administrative Hearings; otherwise, the judgment was affirmed. If the board chose to rehear the matter, board members who participated in settlement negotiations with the pharmacist could not participate in the hearing panel.

 

258 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Miller v. State


No. 90, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


380 Md. 1; 843 A.2d 803; 2004 Md. LEXIS 53



February 19, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected February 20, 2004.
Subsequent civil proceeding at Miller v. Saar, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 152475 (D. Md., Oct. 22, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: Review of Death Penalty. Circuit Court for Allegany County. Case No. 7540. Gary J. Leasure, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: Verdicts and sentences affirmed; death penalty vacated; case remanded for resentencing on murder conviction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Circuit Court for Allegany County (Maryland), which convicted him for premeditated murder, first degree sexual offense, robbery, and false imprisonment, and which imposed a sentence of death for the murder, 30 years in prison for the first degree sexual offense conviction, five years consecutive for the robbery conviction, and one year concurrent for the false imprisonment conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with several offenses related to the death of a babysitter, and the death penalty was sought. Defendant was acquitted of an attempted rape charge, but convicted of all other charges. A jury unanimously concluded, by a preponderance, that the aggravating factor of first degree sexual offense outweighed the mitigators and that defendant should be sentenced to death for murder. Additional prison terms were ordered on the other crimes. On appeal, defendant did not contest that he strangled the babysitter or engaged in sexual activity with her. He argued the sex was consensual, so there was no aggravator for the death penalty. He also challenged his interrogation, use of his confessions, and the jury instructions. The court found that even though defendant's initial interrogation was possibly tainted, his later waiver of Miranda was valid, so the confessions were properly admitted. The jury instructions were all proper as well. Finally, the court found there were three admissions, plus forensic evidence, establishing that sexual activity of some kind occurred and there was medical evidence indicating the activity may have been violent and non-consensual.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed all of the guilty verdicts, and the prison sentences imposed for the first degree sexual offense, robbery, and false imprisonment convictions. However, the court reversed the death sentence and remanded for a new sentencing proceeding on the murder conviction.

 

259 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Melton v. State


No. 61, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


379 Md. 471; 842 A.2d 743; 2004 Md. LEXIS 42



February 12, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Baltimore County). Case # 01CR00864. Lawrence R. Daniels, JUDGE.
Melton v. State, 377 Md. 111, 832 A.2d 204, 2003 Md. LEXIS 627 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Court of Special Appeals, which affirmed a trial judge's decision convicting him of three separate counts of the unlawful possession of a firearm under former Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 445(d)(i), (ii), and (iii) (2001) for possessing a single regulated firearm on a single occasion and sentencing him to three separate sentences pursuant to former Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 449(e) and (f) (2001).


OVERVIEW: Defendant's three separate possession convictions were based on his status as a person previously convicted of a crime of violence, a person previously convicted of a felony, and a person previously convicted of a misdemeanor with a penalty of over two years of incarceration. Reversing in part, the court held that the legislature did not intend for a court to render separate multiple verdicts of convictions for illegal possession of a regulated firearm where defendant fit within several categories of prior qualifying convictions, but only possessed a single regulated firearm on a single occasion. Relying on state and federal caselaw, the construction of the recodified provisions of Md. Ann. Code art. 27, §§ 445, 449 (Md. Code Ann., Pub. Safety §§ 5-101(g) and 5-133 (2003)), and the rule of lenity, the court concluded that the phrase "Each violation shall be considered a separate offense" in Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 449(e) referred to each current act of illegal possession, rather than to each prior qualifying felony conviction. Thus, only one of defendant's convictions under Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 445(d)(1), specifically his conviction under § 445(d)(1)(i), could stand.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for possession based on his status as a person previously convicted of a crime of violence, but it reversed defendant's convictions based on his status as a person previously convicted of a felony and based on his status as a person previously convicted of a misdemeanor with a penalty of over two years of incarceration, and it remanded to the appellate court.

Atty. Griev. Comm'n v. Lichtenberg


Misc. Docket AG No. 67, September Term, 2002


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


379 Md. 335; 842 A.2d 11; 2004 Md. LEXIS 35



February 11, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at Atty. Griev. Comm'n v. Davis, 379 Md. 361, 842 A.2d 26, 2004 Md. LEXIS 37 (2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Circuit Court for Baltimore County Circuit Court for Baltimore County. Case No. 03-C-03-002820. Case No. 3-C-03-002920. Nelson W. Rupp, Jr., JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: Petition for disciplinary action dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Attorney Grievance Commission, through Bar Counsel, brought exceptions and objections to the findings and conclusions rendered by a hearing judge in the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland), who found that respondent attorney did not commit any violations of the rules of professional conduct to warrant disciplinary action.


OVERVIEW: The Commission filed a petition for disciplinary action against the attorney. The court referred the matter to a circuit court for an evidentiary hearing. The hearing judge concluded that neither the rules of professional conduct, nor any other provisions of the Maryland code, were violated. The Commission filed objections. The court found the heart of the complaint was that the attorney violated Md. R. Prof. Conduct 1.15, 8.4, and Md. Code Ann. Ins. § 22-103 (2003), which governed trust brokers' deposits of trust money, by depositing the interest from funds entrusted to him by his trust brokerage clients into his title insurance company's account without the express consent of the beneficial owners. The attorney responded that he could gain consent from either the client or the beneficial owner. It was undisputed that he had the client's consent. The court found the attorney was acting as a broker, not an attorney, and the Insurance Commissioner did not discipline his actions. Also, clear and convincing evidence did not exist that the attorney committed a criminal act in violation of the rules of professional conduct. Therefore, the petition was dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition against the attorney seeking to impose disciplinary action.

 

261 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Edwards Sys. Tech. v. Corbin


No. 102, September Term, 2002


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


379 Md. 278; 841 A.2d 845; 2004 Md. LEXIS 31



February 10, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Prince George's County). James J. Lombardi, JUDGE.
Edwards Sys. v. Corbin, 372 Md. 429, 813 A.2d 257, 2002 Md. LEXIS 979 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certiorari review to an appeal by defendants, an employer and a manager, from a judgment of the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland that reversed a dismissal, on grounds that an enabling statute, Md. Ann. Code art. 49B, § 42 (2003), violated Md. Const. art. XI-A in an action by plaintiff employee seeking to recover for employment discrimination that violated Prince George's County, Md., Code § 2-222 (1999).


OVERVIEW: Three of the counties that enjoyed home rule rights under Md. Const. art. XI-A to enact legislation so long as they enacted only local laws were further explicitly empowered by Md. Ann. Code art. 49B, § 42 to enact anti-discrimination legislation. The county where the employee worked out of an office for her employer enacted such ordinances, and she claimed that the employer, through her manager, discriminated against her in many ways that were forbidden by ordinance. The high court held that all the constitutional provision did was forbid the enactment of statewide public laws, but the ordinance could be construed as applying only to discriminatory acts in the county of enactment by an employer with a significant presence there. The fact that new causes of action were customarily created in other ways did not preclude the county from authorizing a lawsuit. Thus, the law was a local one, authorized by a statute that did not have to be construed as allowing counties to enact laws of statewide application. Moreover, § 42 was not a forbidden local law because it applied to more than one county.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the intermediate court that reversed the dismissal.

Bank of Am. v. Stine


Misc. No. 3, September Term, 2001


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


379 Md. 76; 839 A.2d 727; 2003 Md. LEXIS 832



December 30, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Adopted by Stine v. Nationsbank (In re Stine), 360 F.3d 455, 2004 U.S. App. LEXIS 4822 (4th Cir. Md., Mar. 15, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
Bank of Am., N.A. v. Stine (In re Stine), 252 B.R. 902, 2000 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 13794 (D. Md., 2000)



DISPOSITION: Certified question answered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States Circuit Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit certified the following question to the state supreme court: whether, pursuant to Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 11-504 (1998), a debtor in bankruptcy could claim, as exempt from his bankruptcy estate, wages previously garnished by a judgment creditor, pursuant to Md. Code Ann., Com. Law §§ 15-601 through 15-607 (2000).


OVERVIEW: A bank sued a debtor in a Maryland court, and obtained a judgment awarding it money damages. After it obtained that judgment, the bank filed a writ of garnishment to enforce its judgment, and it attached 25 percent of the debtor's wages. In December 1998, the debtor filed a petition for relief under Chapter 7 of the United States Bankruptcy Code, 11 U.S.C.S. § 701 et. seq., and sought recovery of $ 1,064 which was withheld from his wages and paid to the bank under the writ of garnishment during the 90-day period before he filed his petition for bankruptcy. The United States Circuit Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit asked the state supreme court to determine whether, under Maryland's exemption scheme, the debtor was entitled to recover the amount withheld from his wages. The state supreme court interpreted Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 11-504 (1998), and held that, when the debtor filed for bankruptcy, the wage attachment became a preferential transfer in the form of earned wages, which was recoverable by the trustee in bankruptcy and, derivatively, by the debtor.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court answered the question asked by the federal appellate court.

Town of Easton v. PSC of Md.


No. 28, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


379 Md. 21; 838 A.2d 1225; 2003 Md. LEXIS 825



December 19, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Talbot County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Case # 20-C-02-004552 AA. William S. Horne, JUDGE.
Easton v. PSC, 376 Md. 49, 827 A.2d 112, 2003 Md. LEXIS 388 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Town of Easton (Maryland) filed an action against respondent Public Service Commission of Maryland, seeking judicial review of a decision by the Commission which denied its application for permission to provide electric service to an area it annexed. The Circuit Court for Talbot County (Maryland) affirmed the Commission's decision, and the Town appealed. The state supreme court issued a writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: Thirty-five years after the Public Service Commission of Maryland allowed a cooperative corporation to provide electric service to persons who lived in a designated area of the state, the Town of Easton annexed 217.1 acres of land that was located in the area where the corporation operated. In March 2001, the Town filed a petition with the Commission, seeking permission to provide electric service to the persons who lived in the area that was annexed. The Commission denied the petition, however, and the trial court affirmed the Commission's decision. The state supreme court held that (1) residents who lived in the area that was annexed did not have a fundamental right to receive electric service from the Town; (2) Md. Code Ann., Pub. Util. Co. § 7-210(d) (1998) gave the Commission power to determine if the corporation's service area should be modified because the Town annexed land that was located in the service area; and (3) the record supported the Commission's judgment that the public interest would not be served by transferring electric service rights in the area that was annexed from the corporation to the Town.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

Frase v. Barnhart


No. 6, September Term, 2003


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


379 Md. 100; 840 A.2d 114; 2003 Md. LEXIS 820



December 11, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel Corrected December 15, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Caroline County). Case No. 7764. Karen A.M. Jensen, JUDGE.
Frase v. Barnhart, 374 Md. 82, 821 A.2d 370, 2003 Md. LEXIS 190 (2003)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant mother appealed from an order of the Circuit Court for Caroline County (Maryland) refusing to strike conditions imposed on an earlier order of that court in favor of the mother in a custody dispute with plaintiff temporary caregivers for two of the mother's children. The mother argued that the decision violated her due process rights, that she should have been provided counsel, and that a special master should have recused herself.


OVERVIEW: The mother's drug and alcohol troubles had led to a short incarceration during a period when the maternal grandmother was unable to look after the children, and they stayed with friends of the grandmother. When the mother returned, after six weeks, the friends sought to keep the younger child. By the time a master made findings, the mother had satisfactorily addressed her substance abuse problems, and the trial court adopted the master's recommendations that the mother receive custody, conditioned on seeking housing in a particular family shelter community and on allowing visitation with a sibling who remained with the friends, at their home. The high court held, first, that even though the mother had not timely appealed the original order, she had timely appealed the motion to strike the conditions, and since it interfered with her exercise of custody, it was immediately appealable under Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 12-303. The high court further held that custody awards to fit parents had to be accorded final effect. The conditions themselves violated the mother's due process guarantees, under the Troxel decision, to raise her children as she saw fit.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order denying emergency relief from the conditions, and remanded the matter to the trial court with instructions to modify all conditions attached to its previous custody award. All other issues were moot.

Piscatelli v. Bd. of Liquor License Comm'rs


No. 85, September Term, 2002


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


378 Md. 623; 837 A.2d 931; 2003 Md. LEXIS 812



December 9, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Evelyn O. Cannon, JUDGE.
Piscatelli v. Bd. of Liquor, 371 Md. 613, 810 A.2d 961, 2002 Md. LEXIS 877 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The city board of liquor license commissioners found that appellant tavern owner violated Md. Ann. Code art. 2B, § 11-304(d)(2) (2001) and imposed penalties consisting of fines or, in the alternative, a suspension. The owner appealed. The Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland) affirmed the decision. The owner appealed to the appellate court, but a writ of certiorari was issued.


OVERVIEW: The owner argued that Md. Ann. Code art. 2B, § 11-304(d)(2) relating to ceasing operations, serving food, and providing entertainment between 2 a.m. and 6 a.m., violated Md. Const. art. XI-A, known as the Home Rule Amendment. The owner also presented a statutory interpretation issue concerning the applicability of Md. Ann. Code art. 2B, § 11-304(d)(2) and a federal constitutional challenge to Md. Ann. Code art. 2B, § 11-304(d)(2) based upon the First Amendment and the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. The reviewing court found that, under Md. Ann. Code 2B, § 11-304(d)(2), the owner was a licensee with a B-D-7 license and had to cease all operations between 2 a.m. and 6 a.m. Further, the entertainment offered by tavern was dancing to music provided by disc jockeys, and as such it was not activity protected by the First Amendment. Finally, there was a rational basis for the classification contained in Md. Ann. Code 2B, § 11-304(d), which was all that was required under the Fourteenth Amendment's Equal Protection Clause.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the circuit court was affirmed.

Sadler v. Dimensions Healthcare Corp.


No. 12, September Term, 2002


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


378 Md. 509; 836 A.2d 655; 2003 Md. LEXIS 764



November 26, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected December 15, 2003. As Corrected December 3, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Prince George's County) James J. Lombardi JUDGE. Case No. 99-13905.
Sadler v. Dimensions Health Corp., 141 Md. App. 715, 787 A.2d 807, 2001 Md. App. LEXIS 200 (2001)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a doctor, appealed, by writ of certiorari, from the affirmance by the Maryland Court of Special Appeals of a trial court's entry of summary judgment for respondents, a hospital and the parties to the hospital's decision denying the doctor privileges, in the doctor's suit alleging breach of contract and several torts, and seeking a declaratory judgment.


OVERVIEW: The hospital argued that its credentialing decision should be upheld if it was supported by substantial evidence and made in conformity with its bylaws. The state's highest court held that if the hospital's decision was made in conformity with its legal bylaws, its action was entitled to the deference due any internal corporate decision. If there was not a dispute as to the material fact that the hospital followed its bylaws, the hospital might be entitled to summary judgment under the standard set out in the civil procedure rules. The hospital's argument that as a private, non-profit hospital it was a quasi-public entity, and its decision should be given the deference accorded to an administrative agency's action, was rejected. Credentialing decisions by a private hospital did not constitute administrative agency action and were not subject to substantial evidence review. However, the hospital had the right to control its staffing procedures without the trial court's interference. If the gravamen of the doctor's action was the credentialing decision itself, and a resolution would require a determination of whether the decision was right or fair, the action simply would not lie.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate court was reversed. The case was remanded to the intermediate court with instructions to reverse the judgment of the trial court and to remand the case to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with the opinion.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee appealed the termination of her employment by respondent state department. The administrative law judge affirmed the decision to terminate the employee. Oken v. State


No. 117, September Term, 2002


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


378 Md. 179; 835 A.2d 1105; 2003 Md. LEXIS 750



November 17, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected December 18, 2003. As Corrected December 11, 2003.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Oken v. Maryland, 158 L. Ed. 2d 632, 124 S. Ct. 2084, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 3179 (U.S., 2004)
Subsequent civil proceeding at, Stay granted by Oken v. Sizer, 321 F. Supp. 2d 658, 2004 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 10881 (D. Md., June 14, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Death Penalty - Review of Sentence. John Grason Turnbull, II, JUDGE.
Oken v. State, 367 Md. 191, 786 A.2d 691, 2001 Md. LEXIS 942 (2001)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED, WITH COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After defendant's conviction in the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland) for first degree murder, and resulting death sentence, was affirmed, he moved to correct an illegal/irregular sentence, asserting Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 412 et seq., under which he was sentenced to death, was unconstitutional. The trial court denied the motion, and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed the statutory scheme under which he was sentenced to death was unconstitutional because it allowed a jury to impose that sentence by finding by a preponderance of the evidence, rather than beyond a reasonable doubt, that aggravating circumstances outweighed mitigating circumstances. The court of appeals held a United States Supreme Court decision requiring a jury to decide, beyond a reasonable doubt, if a defendant was eligible to receive the death penalty applied only to the eligibility phase of a capital sentencing proceeding, wherein the jury decided if a statutorily specified aggravating circumstance, making defendant eligible for the death penalty, was shown beyond a reasonable doubt. This requirement was complied with in defendant's case, as a required aggravating circumstance was found beyond a reasonable doubt. This principle did not apply to the phase of the capital sentencing proceeding where the jury decided if the death penalty should be imposed by weighing aggravating circumstances against mitigating circumstances. The State was constitutionally free to allow this determination to be made by a preponderance of the evidence.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

Polk v. State


No. 101, September Term, 2002


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


378 Md. 1; 835 A.2d 575; 2003 Md. LEXIS 746



November 12, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected November 17, 2003.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Polk v. Md., 158 L. Ed. 2d 382, 124 S. Ct. 1691, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 2258 (U.S., Mar. 22, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Circuit Court for Wicomico County. Case No. K0011016.
Polk v. State, 372 Md. 429, 813 A.2d 257, 2002 Md. LEXIS 978 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certiorari review to defendant's appeal from an affirmance, by the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland, of her conviction of disorderly conduct in violation of Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 121(b)(3) (1996 & 2001 Supp.), recodified at Md. Code Ann., Crim. § 10-201(c)(3) (2002) and resisting arrest. She argued that the evidence was insufficient to convict her because she had been engaged in protected speech.


OVERVIEW: When defendant went to the public hospital where she had formerly worked to collect her final paycheck, a mix-up sent her back and forth between departments, and when a hospital police officer finally brought the check but insisted on taking it to human resources first, she exploded in loud obscenities. When she refused to lower her voice, he finally arrested her. The high court found no clear error in the trial court's determination that defendant had violated § 121. Maryland courts looked to three factors in determining whether a limitation on speech was permissible: whether the regulation was content-based or neutral, the surrounding circumstances, including location, and whether less disruptive alternatives were available. There was plenty of evidence that the officer's actions were directed to the volume of defendant's tirade, not the obscene language. The incident in occurred in a hospital, where people expected their privacy to be respected. Finally, defendant could have delivered the same words in a lower voice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions.
