Coleman v. Anne Arundel County Police Dep't


No. 34, September Term, 2001


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


369 Md. 108; 797 A.2d 770; 2002 Md. LEXIS 229



May 6, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County). Case # C-1998-47559. Phillip T. Caroom, JUDGE.

Coleman v. Anne Arundel County Police Dep't, 136 Md. App. 419, 766 A.2d 169, 2001 Md. App. LEXIS 16, 6 Wage & Hour Cas. 2d (BNA) 1337 (2001).



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a police officer, appealed, by a writ of certiorari, from the Maryland Court of Special Appeals' affirmance of a circuit court's affirmance of his termination by a county police chief, alleging that the wrong standard of review was applied under the Law Enforcement Officers' Bill of Rights (LEOBR), Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 727 et seq. (1996, Supp. 1998). Respondent, a county police department, filed a conditional cross-petition.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that the appropriate standard of proof in an action under the LEOBR was a preponderance of the evidence, even for the theft-related offenses charged. Everett v. Balt. Gas & Elec. Co., 307 Md. 286 (1986) was overruled. The officer was afforded the panoply of rights provided by the LEOBR. The LEOBR procedures adequately protected the officer against an erroneous deprivation of his due process claims. Implementation of a heightened standard of proof would not so significantly reduce the risk of error that it should have been implemented regardless of any additional administrative or financial burdens it would entail. A preponderance of the evidence standard, added to the array of other protections under the LEOBR, struck the appropriate balance between protecting the private interests of an officer accused of misconduct and a law enforcement agency's interest in maintaining internal discipline. Thus, the correct evidentiary standard to be applied under LEOBR in local police agency cases was the preponderance of the evidence standard.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

309 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Gray v. State


No. 37, September Term, 2001


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


368 Md. 529; 796 A.2d 697; 2002 Md. LEXIS 159



April 11, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Circuit Court for Charles County Case: Criminal No. 97-156. Christopher C. Henderson, Judge.

Gray v. State, 137 Md. App. 460, 769 A.2d 192, 2001 Md. App. LEXIS 59 (2001).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the trial court of first-degree murder, and that conviction was affirmed by the Maryland Court of Special Appeals. Defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant said someone else was the murderer, but the trial court ruled it could not make this person assert his privilege against self-incrimination before the jury. Before the jury, the person stated his name and birth date, stood next to defendant, and was excused. The trial court refused to instruct the jury that the witness had exercised his right to remain silent and refused to admit inculpatory statements the witness made to another person, finding the declarant unreliable. The court of appeals ruled the trial court erred by not admitting the inculpatory statements against penal interest and allowing the jury to determine the declarant's credibility. The trial court should have determined if defendant would be unfairly prejudiced by not allowing him to call the potentially exculpatory witness who would invoke his self-incrimination privilege in the jury's presence. If the trial court found there was sufficient evidence the proposed witness might have committed the crime, it had discretion to allow defendant to call the witness, and, if it did not allow the witness to be called, it should have instructed the jury that the witness invoked his self-incrimination privilege.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of special appeals was reversed.

 

310 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Motor Vehicle Admin. v. Atterbeary


No. 76, September Term, 2001


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


368 Md. 480; 796 A.2d 75; 2002 Md. LEXIS 157



April 9, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Circuit Court for Howard County. Lenore R. Gelfman, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court for Howard County (Maryland) reversed an administrative law judge's decision that respondent motorist refused to take a breathalyzer test. Petitioner motor vehicle administration appealed.


OVERVIEW: The motorist agreed to a breathalyzer test and was asked to sign a form indicating his consent. After reviewing the form, he asked to speak to an attorney, but had no specific attorney to contact. His response to each question seeking routine information was to ask for an attorney. The officer indicated his refusal to take the test. The court of appeals held he had, on request, to be permitted a reasonable opportunity to consult counsel, as long as this would not substantially interfere with the timely and efficacious administration of the test. He did not have to execute any writing to consent to testing. His decision to exercise his due process right to contact counsel was not necessarily a conditional nor a per se refusal to submit to the test, and his exercise of this right was distinct from an assessment of whether he refused the test. His presence in his vehicle, sitting in the driver's seat, awake, and with the engine running, indicated he could attempt to drive the vehicle and was in control of it, for purposes of the statutory definition of the term "drive" in Md. Code Ann., Transp. § 11-114.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

 

311 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Richard Roeser Prof'l Builder v. Anne Arundel County


No. 79, September Term, 2001


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


368 Md. 294; 793 A.2d 545; 2002 Md. LEXIS 92



March 7, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County) reversed the Board and the Court of Special Appeals reversed the Circuit Court. We reverse the Court of Special Appeals and hold that the Board's application of self-created hardship, based upon the purchase of property, was an erroneous conclusion of law. Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County. Case # C-1999-58456. Joseph P. Manck, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowner sought variances, which were denied by the board of appeals (board) of respondent county, because the landowner knew of the zoning restrictions when he purchased the land. The first reviewing court reversed. The Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) then reversed the first reviewing court, and entered judgment ordering that the decision of the board be reinstated. The landowner's petition for writ of certiorari was granted.


OVERVIEW: The landowner purchased the property aware of property restrictions for the area. The board ruled that the purchase, with knowledge, precluded the granting of a variance, because it was considered a self-created hardship. In disagreeing, the highest appellate court found that while a purchaser of property acquired no greater right to a variance than his predecessor, he also did not acquire less. The right to a variance was not affected by a sale of the land, as to hold otherwise would be inconsistent with the general property-law goal of preserving alienability. The type of hardship that would normally be considered to be self-created did not arise from the purchase of the property, but rather from the actions of a landowner himself that created the hardship. In the instant case, the property was in a "buffer" zone adjacent to wetlands. The landowner, when purchasing the property, knew that variances from the county's zoning provisions would be required in order to build the house it desired. It was error for the court below to find that the purchase was a self-created hardship, since the landowner did nothing to create the situation, other than purchase the property.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed. The matter was remanded, with instructions to the board of appeals.

J. L. Matthews, Inc. v. Maryland-National Capital Park & Planning Comm'n


No. 65, September Term, 2001


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


368 Md. 71; 792 A.2d 288; 2002 Md. LEXIS 86



March 5, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Montgomery County). Case # 208820. Nelson W. Rupp, Jr. JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent planning commission filed for condemnation seeking to acquire a parcel of land from petitioner developer. The commission obtained a preliminary injunction preventing construction activity on the property. The trial court determined the value of the land subject to its denial of certain evidence relating to lost profits. The developer appealed the decision of the Maryland Court of Special Appeals which affirmed the trial court.


OVERVIEW: The commission wanted to convert the parcel of land into a park. The developer had declined the commission's offers and began construction on the parcel. The commission obtained the injunction based upon its assertion that the developer was tearing out trees, which irreparably damaged the parcel. In addition it asserted allowing development to continue would have increased its cost to obtain the land. The appellate court held the lower appellate court erred, as the commission did not have "quick take" authority; rather, it had "regular" authority. Thus, the trial court erred in granting the temporary restraining order and preliminary injunction. The trial court's grant of the commission's second motion in limine wrongfully precluded the developer from presenting evidence that may have affected the jury's assessment of the fair market value of the land at the time of trial. The developer's right to develop the property and to just compensation greatly outweighed the commission's speculative showing as to its interest in retaining the affected trees and vegetation.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the judgments of the court of special appeals and the trial court.

State Ret. & Pension Sys. of Md. v. Thompson


No. 74, September Term, 2001


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


368 Md. 53; 792 A.2d 277; 2002 Md. LEXIS 87



March 4, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Prince George's County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Case No. CAL 96-17152. Robert J. Woods, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with instructions to dismiss complaint.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the State Retirement and Pension System of Maryland (SRPS), obtained a writ of certiorari and a stay of a writ of mandamus issued by the Circuit Court of Prince George's County (Maryland) in its review of a decision of the workers' compensation commission.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that respondent, a workers' compensation claimant who retired as a result of his disability, failed to exhaust his administrative remedies by failing to request a hearing on the decision of the SRPS to offset the claimant's workers' compensation benefits against his disability retirement benefits. As a result, the reviewing court never should have entertained the claimant's petition for mandamus. Furthermore, the reviewing court's orders permitting the claimant to collect both workers' compensation and disability retirement benefits was in contravention of the statutory mandate contained in Md. Code Ann., State Pers. & Pens. § 29-118(b)(1). Government employees covered by both workers' compensation and a pension or retirement plan providing equivalent disability benefits could only obtain a single recovery for a single injury. The reviewing court erred in considering the dire financial straits of the claimant; it was wholly impermissible for the reviewing court to order duplicative payments in the hope that a lump sum settlement of the workers' compensation award could be reached.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the reviewing court was reversed and the case was remanded to the reviewing court with instructions to dismiss the complaint.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed to an intermediate court from a judgment of the Calvert County Circuit Court (Maryland) sentencing defendant to less than the sentence mandated by Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 286(d) (1970, 1996, 2001 Supp.), and the instant court granted certiorari on its own motion.


OVERVIEW: Md. Ann. Defendant cross-appealed that he was entitled to jury determination of his sentencing enhancement, but the court held that a prior prison term was akin to a prior conviction, an enhanced sentencing factor not covered by the constitutional rule of Apprendi.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded with instructions to vacate the sentence previously imposed and to resentence defendant in accordance with the court's opinion.

Baker v. State


No. 40, September Term, 2001


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


367 Md. 648; 790 A.2d 629; 2002 Md. LEXIS 44



February 7, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied, Stay denied by Baker v. Maryland, 535 U.S. 1050, 152 L. Ed. 2d 817, 122 S. Ct. 1814, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 3215 (2002)
Post-conviction relief denied at Baker v. State, 2003 Md. LEXIS 706 (Md., Oct. 17, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of Denial of Post Trial Motions in the Circuit Court for Harford County. Case # 92C0088PC. Cypert O. Whitfill JUDGE.
Baker v. Corcoran, 220 F.3d 276, 2000 U.S. App. LEXIS 17315 (4th Cir. Md., 2000)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in 1992, after a jury trial, of first degree murder with a deadly weapon and the use of a handgun in the commission of a felony. After electing to be sentenced by the court and not the jury, he was sentenced to death. Circuit Court for Harford County (Maryland) judgments denied his motions for new sentencing based on newly discovered evidence and mistake or irregularity. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The jury found beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant was guilty of first degree murder and also, upon defendant's specific request that it address the issue, found beyond a reasonable doubt defendant was the principal actor, an aggravating circumstance that made defendant eligible for the death penalty. Defendant was not able to show any mitigating circumstances that outweighed the aggravating circumstances by a preponderance of the evidence (previously held to be a constitutional standard); therefore, the circuit court was authorized to sentence defendant to death. Apprendi did not apply to Maryland's sentencing scheme since death was the maximum penalty provided for by the statute governing first degree murder and so the death sentence was not an enhancement of the maximum penalty. The indictment was a valid indictment for a capital case. Defendant was substantially provided with the information of the charges and that the State was seeking the death penalty. Defendant was sufficiently advised before making his voluntary waiver of his right to be sentenced by the jury. Defendant's newly discovered evidence would not have produced a different sentence.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals affirmed the judgments.

 

316 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Kassama v. Magat


No. 38, September Term, 2001


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


368 Md. 113; 792 A.2d 1102; 2002 Md. LEXIS 39



February 5, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Baltimore County). Case No. 03-C-97-007112. Robert E. Cadigan, JUDGE.

Kassama v. Magat, 136 Md. App. 637, 767 A.2d 348, 2001 Md. App. LEXIS 24 (2001).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mother filed a medical malpractice action in her own right and in behalf of her daughter against defendant obstetrician. The Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland) dismissed the claims filed in behalf of the daughter and a jury found that the mother had contributed to causing her own injury. The Court of Special Appeals of Maryland affirmed the trial court's judgment, and the state supreme court granted leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: A mother filed a malpractice action, alleging that an obstetrician failed to inform her that blood test results indicated a heightened possibility her unborn child had Down's Syndrome. Evidence showed that the mother's doctor referred her to the obstetrician in February 1995, but she did not make an appointment to see him until March 29, 1995. When the obstetrician saw her on April 19, 1995, he ordered an alpha-fetoprotein blood test. However, the mother did not have that test done until May 16, 1995, and the obstetrician did not receive the test results until May 25, 1995. By the time the obstetrician received the test results and discussed them with the mother, she was too far into her pregnancy to obtain an abortion in Maryland, although she could have obtained an abortion in New York, Kansas, or Arkansas. The mother carried the child to term and it was born with Downs Syndrome. The state supreme court held that (1) the facts supported the jury's verdict that the mother's negligence contributed to the delay in detecting Downs Syndrome; and (2) Maryland would follow the majority of states which do not allow parents to sue in behalf of a child for wrongful life.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's judgment.

 

317 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Robinson v. Bunch


No. 111, September Term, 1998


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


367 Md. 432; 788 A.2d 636; 2002 Md. LEXIS 5



January 11, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Baltimore City). Joseph H.H. Kaplan, JUDGE.

Bunch v. Robinson, 122 Md. App. 437, 712 A.2d 585, 1998 Md. App. LEXIS 134, 4 Wage & Hour Cas. 2d (BNA) 1300, 135 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P 33693 (1998) 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the appeals court was reversed and the case was remanded to that court to affirm the judgment of the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employees and former employee of a state agency sued the Secretary of the Maryland Department of Public Safety and Correctional Services for monetary damages based on their allegations that they were not paid for overtime work in excess of 40 hours per week. They asserted that the failure to pay them for overtime work violated the federal Fair Labor Standards Act (FLSA), 29 U.S.C.S. §§ 201-219.


OVERVIEW: The employees, after being dismissed from federal court, brought suit in state court. The employees stated their action was authorized by two provisions of the FLSA, namely 29 U.S.C.S. §§ 215(a)(3), 216(b). They alternatively contended that their action was authorized by Maryland law. The secretary moved to dismiss, arguing that the state's sovereign immunity barred the federal causes of action and that congress had no authority to abrogate that immunity by authorizing suits of that nature against a state official in state courts. The trial court agreed with the secretary. The lower Court of Special Appeals reversed the trial court. The higher appellate court then rejected the secretary's contention that the issues of Maryland law, added by its order, should not be reached. Covered state employees were entitled to overtime compensation in accordance with applicable state law or the FLSA, whichever was greater. There was a duty on the part of the state or the appropriate officials to make such payments or provide for them through the budget process, and sovereign immunity was not a defense.


OUTCOME: The appellate court held Maryland provided a statutory administrative and judicial review remedy for adjudication of the employees' claims and the remedy was exclusive. Because the lawsuit was not encompassed by the exclusive statutory administrative and judicial review remedy, the trial court correctly dismissed the action.

 

318 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Carter v. State


No. 35, September Term, 2001


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


367 Md. 447; 788 A.2d 646; 2002 Md. LEXIS 6



January 11, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Frederick County). G. Edward Dwyer, Jr. and Mary Ann Stepler, JUDGES.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was arrested for possession of marijuana, in violation of Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 287. She moved to suppress a marijuana cigarette seized following her arrest on an unrelated, outstanding warrant. The motion to suppress was denied, and she was convicted, as charged. Defendant appealed, and the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) entered judgment, affirming her conviction. Defendant filed a petition for a writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: A marijuana cigarette was found inside of a pack of cigarettes, which was inside defendant's lunch bag. The higher appellate court found that the search of the lunch bag was a lawful search incident to arrest. In the instant case, police evidence showed that a copy of the arrest warrant had been served on defendant. Also, the police had relied on the existence of the warrant. The fact the State did not produce the underlying arrest warrant at the suppression hearing did not obviate the lawfulness of the arrest. In addition, defendant had consented to the search of her lunch bag. Her contention that the police exceeded the scope of what otherwise would have been a lawful search, by searching the open pack of cigarettes within her lunch bag, was also rejected. The higher appellate court found that the search of an inner container, of a container within a container, was lawful where the search of the outside container was lawful. The Fourth Amendment only prohibited unreasonable searches and seizures. The search and seizure in the instant case was reasonable.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Borchardt v. State


No. 55, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


367 Md. 91; 786 A.2d 631; 2001 Md. LEXIS 941



December 13, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Writ of certiorari denied: Borchardt v. Maryland, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 3961 (U.S. May 28, 2002).
Writ of certiorari denied Borchardt v. Maryland, 535 U.S. 1104, 122 S. Ct. 2309, 152 L. Ed. 2d 1064, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 3961 (2002)
Post-conviction proceeding at State v. Borchardt, 2007 Md. LEXIS 1 (Md., Jan. 12, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Review of Death Penalty Sentencing - Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County. Case No. K-1999-2077. Barbara K. Howe, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought direct review of judgments of the Anne Arundel County Circuit Court (Maryland) convicting him on a jury verdict of two counts of first-degree murder and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with stabbing two people to death while committing a robbery. Pursuant to the capital sentencing scheme set forth at Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 413, after he was found guilty of these capital offenses, the jury determined that certain aggravating circumstances were present, then considered defendant's evidence of mitigating factors, and then considered whether the aggravating factors outweighed the mitigating factors by a preponderance of the evidence. The high court held that this proceeding did not violate the constitutional rule of Apprendi, which required facts (other than prior convictions) that increased a sentence beyond the statutory maximum to be found beyond a reasonable doubt, both because the United States Supreme Court had excluded death penalty cases from his opinion and because a death sentence was the statutory maximum for defendant's offense. Defendant's other claims of error were meritless, except for one harmless error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of conviction and sentence.

 

321 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Ridge Heating, Air Conditioning & Plumbing, Inc. v. Brennen


No. 135, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


366 Md. 336; 783 A.2d 691; 2001 Md. LEXIS 788



October 19, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Circuit Court for Baltimore County. Civil No. 98-9913.

Circuit Court for Prince George's County. Case No. CT912002X.

Ridge Heating, Air Conditioning & Plumbing, Inc. v. Brennen, 135 Md. App. 247, 762 A.2d 161, 2000 Md. App. LEXIS 188 (2000) 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellees, homeowners, were granted summary judgment in the suit filed by appellant, a subcontractor, to establish and enforce a mechanic's lien. The Maryland Court of Special Appeals affirmed. The Maryland Court of Appeals granted the subcontractor's petition for writ of certiorari as to whether Md. Code Ann., Real Prop. § 9-104(f)(3) (1998, 2001 Supp.), applied to an addition to an existing single family dwelling.


OVERVIEW: Summary judgment in favor of the homeowners was granted because they had paid the contractor in full. The appellate court found that the mechanic's lien law historically had been construed in the most liberal and comprehensive manner in favor of mechanics and materialmen. The mechanic's lien statute, however, contained a homeowners' exception, that limited the homeowners' liability to a subcontractor for work performed and materials rendered by the subcontractor on a single family dwelling erected on the owner's land for his own residence, to the extent that the owner rendered payment to the contractor. The appellate court reasoned that by the time a mechanic's lien notice was received, the homeowner may already have paid the contractor or have accumulated set-offs or credits exceeding what is owed on the contract. Generally, courts had hesitated to construe the mechanic's lien statute against homeowners. Thus, the appellate court held that Md. Code Ann., Real Prop. § 9-104(f)(3) (1998, 2001 Supp.) applied to an addition to an existing home, as well as the construction of a new home.


OUTCOME: The intermediate appellate court's affirmance of the lower court's grant of summary judgment was affirmed. Costs were to be paid by the subcontractor.

 

322 of 744 DOCUMENTS


In re Franklin P.


No. 4, September Term, 2001


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


366 Md. 306; 783 A.2d 673; 2001 Md. LEXIS 787



October 18, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Case Nos. 100290029, 100290030, 100290031. David B. Mitchell, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: Appeal was dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland) entered an order denying petitioner juvenile's motion to dismiss the criminal charges against him for lack of jurisdiction. The juvenile filed an interlocutory appeal in the appellate court. On its own motion, the state's highest court granted the juvenile's petition for certiorari before judgment and his motion for injunction pending appeal, prior to a determination by the appellate court.


OVERVIEW: On review, the juvenile inquired whether the juvenile court had the authority to rescind an order of waiver of juvenile jurisdiction and, if so, whether the circuit court lacked jurisdiction; and whether an immediate appeal could be taken under the collateral order doctrine from the order denying his motion to dismiss the criminal charges. The state's highest court held that the denial of the juvenile's motion to dismiss was not immediately appealable under the collateral order doctrine, because it did not satisfy at least one of the elements of the four-pronged test, i.e., the element which required it to be effectively unreviewable on appeal from a final judgment. To the contrary, such a denial of a motion to dismiss was completely reviewable on appeal; the Maryland Legislature had so provided. The state's highest court also determined that the juvenile's inquiry as to the appropriateness of the order of waiver of juvenile jurisdiction was not yet reviewable on appeal. This determination was consistent with the Maryland Legislature's intent that a challenge to a waiver of juvenile jurisdiction await the final disposition of the particular matter in the circuit court.


OUTCOME: The juvenile's appeal was dismissed.

 

323 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Scott v. State


No. 143, Sept. Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


366 Md. 121; 782 A.2d 862; 2001 Md. LEXIS 780



October 11, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected October 12, 2001. Certiorari Denied March 18, 2002, Reported at: 2002 U.S. LEXIS 1740.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore County Pursuant to Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Case No. 00-CR-2065. Lawrence R. Daniels, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED, WITH COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, convicted in the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland) of possession with intent to distribute cocaine and, as a repeat offender, sentenced to a prison term of 10 years without parole, appealed his conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought to suppress evidence obtained in a knock and talk where the police obtained entry to his motel room without either a warrant, probable cause to believe, or a reasonable articulable suspicion that there was any illegal activity at the motel. The appellate court was not prepared to find every late-night knock and talk encounter a Fourth Amendment seizure. Nor was the knock and talk a violation of Md. Const. Decl. Rights art. 26, which Maryland courts had consistently construed as being in pari materia with the Fourth Amendment. The voluntariness of consent to a search was a question of fact, to be determined from a totality of the circumstances. The trial court did not commit any error of law or any clear error of fact in determining that defendant consented to the search of his room and that the consent was voluntary. The evidence, in a light most favorable to the State, showed no police overbearing, or even impoliteness. The court refused to follow Washington and New Jersey, which required the police to notify occupants of their right to refuse their consent based on the state constitutions.


OUTCOME: The conviction was affirmed.

 

324 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Bell Atl. of Md., Inc. v. Intercom Sys. Corp.


No. 13, September Term, 2001


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


366 Md. 1; 782 A.2d 791; 2001 Md. LEXIS 779



October 10, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Prince George's County). Thomas P. Smith, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION:  [***1] JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED WITH COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE PETITIONER.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner carrier filed a writ of certiorari to review a judgment in favor of respondent consumer by the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) to determine whether an administrative remedy before the Public Service Commission (PSC) was exclusive, primary, or concurrent. The petition was granted.


OVERVIEW: The consumer, an Internet service provider, filed numerous complaints with the PSC alleging that the carrier's service was inadequate and it engaged in improper billing and discriminatory treatment. The consumer also filed an independent judicial action. The trial court held that the PSC had original jurisdiction and dismissed the action. The appellate court reversed. The state supreme court held that common law causes of action were not abrogated by the administrative procedures. The consumer was required to initially lodge its complaints with the PSC. The consumer's complaint set forth common law causes of action arising out of the carrier's conduct, which could not be resolved by the PSC. Those claims could only be dealt with in an independent action. The consumer could pursue its independent action provided that it exhausted its administrative remedies first.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Galloway v. State


No. 21, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


365 Md. 599; 781 A.2d 851; 2001 Md. LEXIS 615



September 19, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected October 31, 2001. Certiorari Denied April 15, 2002, Reported at: 2002 U.S. LEXIS 2423.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County). Case # K-98-00688. Clayton Greene, Jr., JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED, WITH COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate appealed the judgment of the Special Court of Appeals (Maryland) which affirmed a trial court decision that convicted the inmate on the charge of harassment in violation of Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 123 (2000 Cum. Supp.) for writing letters to his former victim during his incarceration.


OVERVIEW: A prison inmate serving a sentence for stalking and kidnapping a woman, his common law wife, was charged with stalking and harassing her by sending her over 100 letters from prison, despite a court order forbidding him from doing so. The trial judge acquitted him of the stalking charge, but found him guilty of the harassment charge. The inmate appealed, contending that the statute under which he was convicted, Md. Ann Code art. 27, § 123, was unconstitutional, and that there was insufficient evidence to support the conviction. The interim court affirmed, and the inmate again appealed. The appellate court found that whether the statute should be tested for vagueness on its face was immaterial; the statute survived the void-for-vagueness doctrine whether it was scrutinized using the agreed facts in the case alone or employing the imagined facts of marginal cases which were cited by the inmate in opposition to the statute. It was enough that the trial judge found that the harassment elements of the statute had been satisfied.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the special court of appeals was affirmed.

State Ethics Comm'n v. Antonetti


No. 111, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


365 Md. 428; 780 A.2d 1154; 2001 Md. LEXIS 611



September 11, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected September 13, 2001. As Corrected November 5, 2001.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Prince George's County). G. R. Hovey Johnson, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH INSTRUCTIONS TO VACATE THE JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY AND TO REMAND THE CASE TO THE CIRCUIT COURT WITH DIRECTIONS TO AFFIRM THE ORDER OF THE STATE ETHICS COMMISSION. COSTS IN THIS COURT AND THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY RESPONDENT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Maryland Ethics Commission's order decided appellee county board of supervisors of elections administrator did not comply with requirements and limitations of the ethics law by hiring family members and not properly reporting and imposed sanctions. A Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) judgment affirmed a circuit court decision vacating and remanding the commission's order. The commission was granted certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals determined that (1) the record contained substantial evidence to support the commission's decision that the administrator had violated Md. Code Ann., State Gov't § 15-607, by not disclosing employment of family members, and (2) that his conduct with regard to the recruitment, hiring, promoting, and supervising of his family members as employees of the elections board violated the tenets of the conflicts of interest provisions set forth in Md. Code Ann., State Gov't §§ 15-501(a), 15-506. The county elections board was a state agency. The need to investigate and sanction alleged ethical violations was acute. There was no evidence to indicate the board had knowledge of or approved the administrator's family hiring. The hiring and payment were discretionary and not excepted ministerial acts. The position openings were not advertised nor was it shown there was a shortage of workers. The public ethics law prohibited state employees from intentionally using their public office prestige for their private gain. In so doing, the instant court was deferential towards the agency's findings of fact and the inferences drawn by the agency from those facts.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals reversed the court of special appeal's judgment and remanded the case with instructions to vacate the circuit court's judgment and to remand the case to the circuit court with instructions to affirm the commission's order.

Mayor & City Council of Baltimore v. Ross


No. 142, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


365 Md. 351; 779 A.2d 380; 2001 Md. LEXIS 609



September 10, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected September 25, 2001.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Baltimore City). Evelyn O. Cannon, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED; COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE MAYOR AND CITY COUNCIL OF BALTIMORE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed a Court of Special Appeals of Maryland reversal of a trial court's summary declaratory judgment in its favor in plaintiff former employee's action alleging the city's failure to rehire her after layoff violated its own laws, as well as Md. Const. Decl. Rights arts. 19, 24.


OVERVIEW: A city planner was laid off for budgetary reasons. Both Baltimore City, Md., Charter art. VII, § 100(b) and Baltimore City, Md., Civ. Serv. Comm'n R. 39C guaranteed her a priority in rehiring for a year. When vacancies of comparable positions occurred during the year, instead of posting and filling them, the city used a provision in its personnel policies that permitted it to move existing, lower paid employees into the positions on a temporary basis; it then put them into the permanent positions when the former employee's priority hiring period expired. She sought a declaration that the city's actions violated state constitutional guarantees, but the court held instead that under the plain language of the personnel policies, there was a triable issue of fact as to whether moving existing employees into those positions constituted filling a position to which the former employee had a right.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the intermediate appeals court and remanded for further proceedings.

Pickett v. Sears, Roebuck & Co.


No. 104, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


365 Md. 67; 775 A.2d 1218; 2001 Md. LEXIS 459



July 13, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Circuit Court for Howard County. Neil E. Axel, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR HOWARD COUNTY AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant debtor sought review of the judgment of the Howard County Circuit Court (Maryland) which found he was properly served with process and denied his request for a jury trial due to its untimeliness prior to finding in favor of the creditor.


OVERVIEW: The debtor reneged on his credit card agreement. The creditor filed suit and subsequently attempted to obtain service. After repeated attempts failed, the trial court ordered service to be made by posting a copy of the summons and complaint as near as possible to his residence and sending a copy by mail. The debtor subsequently filed a motion to quash which was denied. He then filed a request for jury trial. However, his request was untimely and denied. The appellate court held the debtor's procedural due process rights were not violated by the trial court's method of obtaining service. The "nail and mail" procedure for service was proper since there was no evidence submitted by the debtor to indicate he did not receive notice. The appellate court also held it was proper to deny his request for a jury trial since it fell outside the statutory time limit.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Clark v. State


No. 102, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


364 Md. 611; 774 A.2d 1136; 2001 Md. LEXIS 399



June 26, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Baltimore County). Case 98 CR1404. John Grason Turnbull, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH DIRECTIONS TO REVERSE THE JUDGMENTS OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE COUNTY AND TO REMAND THE CASE TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR A NEW TRIAL. COSTS IN THIS COURT AND THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY BALTIMORE COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed Court of Special Appeals of Maryland affirmance of conviction of first-degree felony murder and related offenses, arguing trial court error in finding that a 15-year delay in charging him did not deny due process and in refusing to permit cross-examination regarding a witness's problems of memory loss.


OVERVIEW: Police did not charge defendant with murder and robbery until 15 years after the incident; he was always a suspect, but there was not quite enough evidence to convict until an accomplice agreed to testify. The high court held that there was no due process denial under either the Maryland or United States principles of due process, because although defendant was certainly prejudiced by the delay, he failed to show that the prosecution delayed with a purpose to prejudice him. Speedy trial rights did not attach until a defendant was charged. However, defendant's due process rights were denied when the trial court abused its discretion in refusing to permit cross-examination of the accomplice regarding memory loss problems that allegedly occurred after an intervening injury. Ability to remember was always relevant, and there was no reason not to permit cross-examination regarding it.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the intermediate court's judgment, and remanded with directions to reverse the trial court's judgments and remand for a new trial.

WASHINGTON SUBURBAN SANITARY COMM'N v. UTILITIES, INC.


No. 116, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


365 Md. 1; 775 A.2d 1178; 2001 Md. LEXIS 393



June 21, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Prince George's County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Case No. CAL97-17811. James J. Lombardi, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY MODIFIED TO AN AWARD OF $ 9.7 MILLION. IN ALL OTHER RESPECTS THE JUDGMENT IS AFFIRMED. COSTS ON THE APPEAL AND CROSS-APPEAL TO BE PAID BY WASHINGTON SUBURBAN SANITARY COMMISSION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Washington Suburban Sanitary Commission (WSSC) condemned water and sewerage systems (systems) owned by defendant utility. The Circuit Court for Prince George's County (Maryland) judgment awarded $ 6.5 million damages, deducting $ 3.2 million in Md. Ann. Code art. 29, § 3-107 contributions in aid of construction (CIAC) from a jury value of $ 9.7 million. The utility and WSSC appealed. WSSC's writ of certiorari petition was granted.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals decided, inter alia, (1) to disallow the CIAC deduction under § 3-107, since the deduction constituted an unlawful taking without just compensation, (2) the jury valuation was supported by evidence of appraisers who opined upon different methodologies, including comparable sales, capitalization of income, and reconstruction cost new less depreciation, and (3) the WSSC's decision to condemn to improve water quality, without proof that the WSSC would achieve that goal, was not arbitrarily or unreasonably made. The valuation issue revolved around the CIAC concept. CIAC was excluded from the utility's rate base to determine utility charges, and to not deduct CIAC would result in customers paying twice for the CIAC component, so the WSSC argued it should be excluded from condemnation valuation. The utility argued that CIAC was part of their property even though derived from purchasers of land or others. The court of appeals defined CIAC and dissected valuation methodologies, and distinguished rate making and condemnation, to decide that CIAC was not to be deducted from the jury award.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals granted the utility's appeal to reverse the judgment deduction of the $ 3.2 million of CIAC and to therefore increase the award to $ 9.7 million, and affirmed the judgment on the appeal of the WSSC.

Tipton v. Partner's Mgmt. Co.


No. 117, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


364 Md. 419; 773 A.2d 488; 2001 Md. LEXIS 387



June 6, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Circuit Court for Baltimore City. Case # 24-C-00-003315/CC3315. Ellen M. Heller, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH INSTRUCTIONS TO REVERSE THE JUDGMENT OF THE DISTRICT COURT OF MARYLAND; COSTS TO BE PAID BY RESPONDENT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner tenant under residential lease appealed affirmance, by Baltimore City Circuit Court (Maryland), of trial court judgment for respondent landlord for rent arrearages, interest, and attorneys' fees, arguing applicable limitations period was three years, under Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 5-101 (1998), although lease was under seal.


OVERVIEW: Nine years after petitioner co-signed a one-year residential lease, respondent landlord sued him for rent arrearages, interest, and fees. Because the lease had the word "seal" printed on it, the trial court held that it was a specialty instrument under Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 5-102 (1998), subject to a 12-year limitations period. Petitioner argued that the action was time-barred by Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 5-101 (1998), setting a three-year period for most contracts. The reviewing court looked for the intent of the legislature, and found that since the 18th century, the three-year period had applied to actions on residential leases. When Maryland statutes were recodified in the 1970s, there was no indication of intent to change this rule, only to clarify those that previously existed. In fact, the codifiers strongly suggested that contracts under seal be abolished. Therefore, respondent's action was time-barred.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded with instructions to reverse the trial court's judgment. Recodification did not affect applicability of a three-year statute to residential leases in the absence of any indication of legislative intent to the contrary.

Dixon v. State


No. 93, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


364 Md. 209; 772 A.2d 283; 2001 Md. LEXIS 315



May 14, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Prince George's County). Case # CT 97-1376X. G.R. Hovey Johnson JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS WITH DIRECTIONS TO VACATE THE SENTENCES IMPOSED BY THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY AND REMAND THE CASE TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR A NEW SENTENCING PROCEEDING NOT INCONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION; COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY, MARYLAND.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's convictions of three crimes were reversed on appeal and he was retried. The State entered a nolle prosequi as to one of the charges, attempted voluntary manslaughter, and a jury again convicted defendant of first degree assault and using a handgun in the commission of a crime of violence. The Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) affirmed on direct appeal. Defendant's petition for writ of certiorari was granted.


OVERVIEW: After the first trial, defendant was sentenced to 20 years in prison for the assault, 10 years for the attempted manslaughter (to be served concurrent with the assault sentence), and 20 years for the handgun offense (to be served consecutively to the assault sentence). After the second trial, defendant was sentenced to 20 years for the assault and 20 years for the handgun violation, to be served consecutively. Defendant argued that sentencing him to 20 years for the assault was illegal as the assault merged with the attempted voluntary manslaughter. The State argued that the sentence was not illegal because there was no net increase in the original sentence. On certiorari, the appellate court held that the attempted manslaughter merged with the assault under Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 12A-1(a)(1) using the required evidence test so that the assault sentence should not have exceeded the attempted manslaughter sentence. Accordingly, after the second trial, the trial court erred in giving a sentence for the assault that was greater than the sentence for attempted manslaughter that was imposed after the first trial, regardless of the nolle prosequi for attempted manslaughter.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the judgment of the court of special appeals, remanded to the court of special appeals with directions to vacate the sentences imposed by the circuit court and remand the case to the circuit court for a new sentencing proceeding consistent with the appellate opinion, and ordered the costs in the court of appeals and the court of special appeals to be paid by the county in which defendant was convicted.

Montrose Christian Sch. Corp. v. Walsh


Nos. 144 & 147, September Term, 1999


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


363 Md. 565; 770 A.2d 111; 2001 Md. LEXIS 132; 85 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 859



April 12, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. DeLawrence Beard, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR MONTGOMERY COUNTY REVERSED, AND BOTH CASES ARE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH DIRECTIONS TO ENTER JUDGMENTS FOR THE DEFENDANTS. COSTS IN NO. 144 TO BE PAID ONE-HALF BY THE PLAINTIFFS AND ONE-HALF BY MONTGOMERY COUNTY. COSTS IN NO. 147 TO BE PAID ONE-HALF BY THE PLAINTIFF CARVER AND ONE-HALF BY MONTGOMERY COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employees sued defendant employers for employment discrimination in violation of Montgomery County, Md., Code § 27-19. The Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland) granted summary judgment to employee in one case. A jury awarded damages to employees in the other case, but the trial court dismissed a defendant in that case. On the parties' petitions, the appellate court issued writs of certiorari and consolidated the cases.


OVERVIEW: Employers were a religious school and its principal. Employees were fired because they did not belong to the church which ran the school, and claimed the firing, based as it was on their religious creed, violated a county ordinance prohibiting employment discrimination on that basis. The supreme court held that the common law doctrine of charitable immunity was not a defense to liability imposed by statute and could not be raised by the employers here. The state employment discrimination statute did not pre-empt the county ordinance. However, the county ordinance as written violated the Free Exercise Clause of the First Amendment as well as Md. Const. Decl. Rts. art. 36. The supreme court found that an offensive clause in the statute which limited the application of an exception for religious organizations could be severed, and struck down the clause, thereby leaving the exception allowing religious organizations to hire or fire on the basis of religious creed.


OUTCOME: The judgments were reversed, and the cases remanded with instructions to enter judgments for the employers.

 

334 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Germain v. State


No. 82, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


363 Md. 511; 769 A.2d 931; 2001 Md. LEXIS 128



April 10, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County). Case # K-1999-0050. Eugene M. Lerner JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS WITH INSTRUCTIONS TO REVERSE THE JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR ANNE ARUNDEL COUNTY AND TO REMAND THE CASE TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR A NEW TRIAL; COSTS TO BE PAID BY ANNE ARUNDEL COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) affirming his conviction. Defendant claimed that the trial court improperly precluded him from using a key prosecution witness's pre-sentence investigation (PSI) for any reason, because it was confidential.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of stabbing his prison cell-mate. Defendant claimed that he acted in self-defense, because his cell-mate was HIV positive and was making sexual advances toward defendant. At trial, defendant called the victim to the stand and asked the victim about his two second degree sex offenses. The victim claimed he could not remember the details of the offenses. The trial court refused to allow defendant to use the PSI to refresh the victim's recollection, because it was confidential. The appellate court reversed. The requirement that a PSI remain confidential was in order to prevent public inspection. Because defendant intended to show it only to the subject for whom it was prepared, the confidentiality of the PSI was not at risk. The trial court's failure to allow defendant to utilize the PSI to refresh the victim's recollection was reversible error.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed. It was improper to preclude defendant from using a key prosecution witness's pre-sentence investigation (PSI) to refresh that witness's recollection. Using a PSI to refresh a witness's memory when the witness has a right to see it was not prohibited by statute.

 

335 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Gigeous v. E. Corr. Inst.


No. 69, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


363 Md. 481; 769 A.2d 912; 2001 Md. LEXIS 144



April 10, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Somerset County). Case # 19-C-99-006734 AA. Daniel M. Long, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED, WITH COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner correction officer filed a petition requesting certiorari review of the judgment of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) affirming the judgment of the Circuit Court for Somerset County which affirmed the administrative termination by the state department of correction of petitioner's employment at respondent correctional institution. The supreme court granted the petition for writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was arrested and charged with the criminal offense of possession of a controlled dangerous substance. Respondent suspended petitioner and ultimately dismissed petitioner from his job. The dismissal was reviewed by three different administrative law judges (ALJ). Petitioner contended his termination was based improperly on the records of his arrest and prosecution that had been expunged according to Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 737. The trial court affirmed petitioner's termination as appropriate because it was founded on the police officers' testimony, based on their personal recollections, and not on expunged records. Upon certiorari review, the supreme court determined that an ALJ did not abuse her discretion when she concluded the officers testified from their independent recollection and that such conclusion was supported by substantial evidence on the record. In addition, the supreme court found the appellate court was correct in determining the issue regarding petitioner's viewing of another ALJ's personnel file was moot because petitioner effectively was afforded the relief to which he might have been entitled had recusal been found to be appropriate.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the judgment.

Redman v. State


No. 39, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


363 Md. 298; 768 A.2d 656; 2001 Md. LEXIS 101

DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY PETITIONER.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed the judgment of the court of special appeals (Maryland) which reversed the judgment of the circuit court, which, on motion of petitioner, granted petitioner a new trial following petitioner's conviction in that court for murder and attempted rape.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was indicted for first degree murder, robbery, attempted first degree rape, and other related offenses. The court of special appeals affirmed the judgments of the circuit court. The circuit court granted a new trial; the court of special appeals reversed, and petitioner appealed. The appellate court found that defense counsel's error in the case was not the type in which prejudice would be presumed. Further, the appellate court found that petitioner had not satisfied the prejudice prong of the Strickland analysis. Finally, trial counsel's failure to inform petitioner of his right to automatic removal, without more, did not necessarily render the criminal trial fundamentally unfair or unreliable. As such, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the special court of appeals was affirmed.

Walker v. State


No. 51, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


363 Md. 253; 768 A.2d 631; 2001 Md. LEXIS 92



March 8, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Case No. 99 CR 3794. John Grason Turnbull, II, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE COUNTY AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in Baltimore County Circuit Court (Maryland) of third-degree sexual assault, Md. Ann. Code §§ 27, 464B(a)(5), (1957, 1996 Repl. Vol.), statutory rape involving a 14-year-old or 15-year-old, arguing that he should have been allowed to raise a reasonable mistake of age defense.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had good reason to believe the young woman he lived with was 17 years-old, but she was actually 15. Because he was over 21, he was prosecuted for third-degree sexual assault in violation of Md. Ann. Code § 464B(a)(5), the statutory rape offense applicable to contact between an adult and a 14- or 15-year-old. He argued that he should have been allowed to raise a defense of reasonable mistake of age. The court used the same reasoning it had used earlier to find the defense unavailable in a prosecution for second-degree sexual assault, sexual contact between younger children and persons four years or more their senior. The legislative history showed that the prohibitions had been drafted specifically to preclude that defense, or was there a violation of due process or right of privacy, the later was often trumped by the state's compelling interest in child welfare.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction. Legislative history clarified that legislators did not intend defense to be available. There was no due process or privacy right violation given the state's compelling interest in child welfare and that it would only reach conduct involving someone almost 16 if the defendant were 21.

White v. State


No. 70, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


363 Md. 150; 767 A.2d 855; 2001 Md. LEXIS 87



March 5, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Wicomico County). Case # K990319. D. William Simpson, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH DIRECTIONS TO REVERSE THE JUDGMENTS OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR WICOMICO COUNTY. COSTS IN THIS COURT AND THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY WICOMICO COUNTY, MARYLAND.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought certiorari review of Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) affirmance of his drug and paraphernalia possession offense and conspiracy convictions, arguing insufficiency of evidence to show knowledge that drugs were present and failure to excuse certain jurors for cause.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was a passenger in a car stopped by police officers. An eventual search of the vehicle led to discovery of a large amount of cocaine and drug paraphernalia in a sealed box in the trunk. The court held that petitioner's presence in the car, without more, was insufficient evidence as a matter of law to support his convictions of drug and paraphernalia possession, drug possession with intent to distribute, and conspiracy. Knowledge was an essential element of all these offenses, not knowledge that drug possession was wrong, which was presumed, but actual knowledge that the drugs were present. Under the circumstances of petitioner's behavior during the stop, together with the manner in which the contraband was hidden, it was not possible for a rational fact-finder to have found guilt beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: Petitioner failed to preserve alleged jury selection error, but the court reversed his convictions because the circumstantial evidence that petitioner, a passenger, had any knowledge of contraband in a sealed container in the trunk was inadequate as a matter of law to support his conviction.

Becker v. State


No. 111, September Term, 1999


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


363 Md. 77; 767 A.2d 816; 2001 Md. LEXIS 84



March 2, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Circuit Court for Baltimore City. Joseph H.H. Kaplan, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED TO THE CIRCUIT COURT WITH DIRECTIONS TO REVERSE THE JUDGMENT OF THE DISTRICT COURT OF MARYLAND AND REMAND THE CASE TO THE DISTRICT COURT FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS NOT INCONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN THE CIRCUIT COURT TO BE PAID BY THE RESPONDENTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The trial court entered an order pursuant to Md. Code Ann., Real Prop. § 14-120, finding that petitioner's property was a nuisance due to its use in connection with illegal drug activity, and ordering that the property be razed at petitioner's expense. The circuit court (Maryland) affirmed the decision. Petitioner filed a petition for a writ of certiorari challenging the decision, which was granted.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was the owner of record of a convenience store. Respondent filed a complaint for abatement of nuisance pursuant to Md. Code Ann., Real Prop. § 14-120, alleging the property was used for the purpose of illegally distributing, storing, and administering controlled dangerous substances. The complaint stated that drug users frequently gathered at the store for the purpose of purchasing and using drugs. The trial court ordered that the property be destroyed. The court held that a district court's authority under Md. Code Ann., Real Prop. § 14-120 did not extend to the destruction of a building which was used for unlawful activity. The court found the unlawful activity at which the statute was aimed did not inhere in a building itself. Instead, it was entirely in the use of the building. The court held that authority to order the destruction of property did not extend to the situation. The court further held that construing Md. Code Ann., Real Prop. § 14-120 as allowing for destruction of property without any compensation to the owner raised serious questions concerning the statute's constitutionality under Md. Const. art. III, § 40 and Md. Const. Decl. Rts. art. 24.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the decision, because destruction of property was not an available remedy where the property was only used for an unlawful activity.

Rawlings v. Rawlings

COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


362 Md. 535; 766 A.2d 98; 2001 Md. LEXIS 19

DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED IN PART AND AFFIRMED IN PART; CASE REMANDED TO THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS WITH DIRECTION TO REVERSE IN PART THE JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR HOWARD COUNTY AND REMAND THE CASE TO THAT COURT FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS NOT INCONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION; COSTS TO BE PAID TWO-THIRDS BY PETITIONER AND ONE-THIRD BY RESPONDENT.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner ex-husband petitioned for a writ of certiorari from a judgment of civil contempt against him in respondent ex-wife's action to enforce a child support order.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner ex-husband was found in civil contempt for failure to comply with a child support order, and ordered to serve jail time, after the trial court set a purge amount. The appellate court affirmed. Petitioner filed a petition for a writ of certiorari, claiming that the trial court erred in applying Md. R. Civ. P. 15-207(e) retroactively, that the lower courts erred in finding him in contempt of the child support order, and that the trial court erred in establishing the purge amount. P. 15-207(e) to petitioner retroactively, and that there was sufficient evidence in the record to hold petitioner in contempt for failure to comply with the child support order. OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed in part because the lower courts did not err in applying the civil contempt rule to petitioner ex-husband retroactively, and there was sufficient evidence in the record to hold petitioner in contempt for failure to comply with the child support order. Judgment was reversed in part because the trial court erred in setting a purge provision and ordering petitioner incarcerated.

Maryland Correctional Institution-Women v. Lee


No. 115, September Term, 1998


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


362 Md. 502; 766 A.2d 80; 2001 Md. LEXIS 12



January 12, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Case No. 97356926. Clifton J. Gordy, Jr., JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY REVERSED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE MAYOR AND CITY COUNCIL OF BALTIMORE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee, a prisoner in appellant correctional facility, filed a petition for habeas corpus, challenging appellant's interpretation of her sentence. The Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland) granted the petition and held that appellee's terms for multiple counts of felony theft were concurrent rather than consecutive. Appellant filed an appeal.


OVERVIEW: Appellee was convicted of nine counts of felony theft and one count of conspiracy. Appellee filed a petition for habeas corpus, contending that appellant correctional facility was required to follow the sentencing court's oral pronouncement of sentence, which conflicted with the written order. The circuit court determined that the state department of corrections had failed to abide by its own procedures for clarification of ambiguous commitments and that it was required to follow the oral pronouncement. As a result, according to the circuit court, appellee's term consisted of 10 concurrent sentences of three years each. The court, holding first that habeas corpus was the appropriate method for appellee to raise the issue, and appellant had the right to appeal the circuit court's decision. The court then held that the circuit court misconstrued appellee's sentence. The intention of the sentencing judge to impose a 24-year sentence was clear. Consequently, there could be no illegal modification of the sentence.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed. Habeas corpus was the appropriate way for appellee to raise the issue of her alleged right to be released from custody, and appellant had the right to appeal the circuit court decision. Appellee's sentence was not ambiguous, and the habeas corpus court erred in modifying the sentence from the 24 years the trial court imposed.

Utilities, Inc. v. Washington Suburban Sanitary Comm'n


No. 101, September Term, 1998


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


362 Md. 37; 763 A.2d 129; 2000 Md. LEXIS 756



December 6, 2000, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Prince George's county pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Sherrie L. Krauser, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION:  [***1] JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY REVERSED, AND CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH DIRECTIONS TO DISMISS THE DECLARATORY JUDGMENT ACTION. EACH PARTY TO PAY ITS OWN COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged Circuit Court for Prince George's County's (Maryland) grant of summary judgment in favor of appellee and declaratory judgment which determined Md. Ann. Code art. 29, § 3-107(a) (1957 & 1997) was applicable to condemnation action by appellee against appellant's property, and was constitutional. The court issued a writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: Appellant filed a declaratory judgment action seeking a determination of the applicability of Md. Ann. Code art. 29, § 3-107(a) (1957 & 1997) to an impending condemnation action by appellee. Appellant's action was filed before condemnation proceedings had begun. The court, sua sponte, raised the issue of whether the case was appropriate for declaratory judgment. The Maryland law did not authorize appellant's declaratory judgment action. A condemnation action could only be initiated by appellee, not by appellant landowner. Appellee had the authority to abandon the action up until the actual taking of the property or 120 days after entry of judgment. Allowing appellant to maintain the declaratory judgment action before a condemnation action was filed, could not be reconciled with the proper role of appellee and appellant with regard to the power of eminent domain.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment on the ground that Maryland law did not authorize a declaratory judgment action by appellant landowner, when appellee had not yet instituted a condemnation action. Condemnation action was exclusive vehicle for judicial resolution of issues relating to just compensation.

 

343 of 744 DOCUMENTS


Richardson v. McGriff


No. 142, September Term, 1999


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


361 Md. 437; 762 A.2d 48; 2000 Md. LEXIS 690



November 15, 2000, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Baltimore City). Robert I.H. Hammerman, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED, WITH COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought certiorari to have the court review judgment of Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) which affirmed jury verdict in favor of respondent police officer on petitioner's claims of battery, gross negligence, and violation of rights under Md. Const. Decl. Rts. art. 26. The court granted certiorari.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, after breaking into a vacant apartment, hid in a closet to avoid police detection. When the police opened the closet, petitioner was shot by respondent officer who mistook a vacuum cleaner hose for a weapon. The trial court properly excluded evidence of police regulations, guidelines, and training procedures pertaining to the use of deadly force, as well as testimony of a superior officer regarding the propriety of respondent's entrance into the apartment. The only relevant information for the jury's consideration was the circumstances at the immediate time of the shooting. The propriety of prior decisions of respondent, that could not have been changed at that point, were not relevant. None of the actions pleaded, and none that were submitted to the jury, were based on violations of orders, regulations, or guidelines. Respondent clearly had a right to be in the apartment, and had a right to search it. The law could not reasonably put respondent in a position, at the split-second of the shooting, of being faced with the impossible choice of either defending himself, or putting his life in immediate mortal danger in order to save his pocketbook.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed. Jury was only entitled to consider circumstances of police shooting as seen by reasonable police officer at time of shooting, rather than to view circumstances with the benefit of 20/20 hindsight. Batson challenge was untimely since it was raised after jury was impaneled and sworn.

Frankel v. Board of Regents of the Univ. of Maryland Sys.


No. 26, September Term, 1999


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


361 Md. 298; 761 A.2d 324; 2000 Md. LEXIS 677



November 6, 2000, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Montgomery County). D. Warren Donohue, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED TO THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS WITH DIRECTIONS TO REVERSE THE JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR MONTGOMERY COUNTY AND REMAND THE CASE TO THAT COURT FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY THE RESPONDENTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought writ of certiorari challenging a judgment of the Maryland Court of Special Appeals holding that the decision to classify petitioner as financially dependent on his non-resident parents for purposes of determining his residency status for payment of tuition was not arbitrary and capricious, illegal, or unreasonable.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was born and raised most of his life in Maryland. He lived year-round while attending the university, was a registered Maryland voter, was employed part-time, paid income taxes to the State of Maryland, and he had a Maryland driver's license. His application for in-state tuition payment status was denied. He was classified as a financially dependent student, and because his parents lived out-of-state he was deemed a nonresident. After graduating he sought a refund of the difference between in-state and out of state tuition. The court held the tuition policy of the Maryland Board of Regents and the board's absolute preclusion of in-state tuition status for any student whose primary monetary support came from an out-of-state source arbitrarily and irrationally discriminated against many bona fide Maryland residents in violation of the equal protection component of Md. Const. Decl. Rts. art. 24. The policy placed in one class bona fide Maryland residents whose primary source of funds was within the state, and placed in another, higher paying class bona fide Maryland residents whose primary source of funds was outside the state.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed. Policy of denying in-state tuition status to Maryland residents who received support from non-residents created an arbitrary and irrational classification which violated equal protection principles. The petitioner was entitled to have his residency classification determined by the university based on the eight "domicile" criteria without using the "financial dependence" and "financial independence" factors.

Heron v. Strader


No. 13, September Term, 2000 No. 13, September Term, 2000


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


361 Md. 258; 761 A.2d 56; 2000 Md. LEXIS 664



October 17, 2000, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Ceriorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Prince George's County). Civil No. CAL98-10872. Arthur M. Ahalt, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED IN PART AND REVERSED IN PART. CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH INSTRUCTIONS TO AFFIRM THE JUDGMENT OF DISMISSAL OF PETITIONER'S CLAIMS OF FALSE ARREST AND FALSE IMPRISONMENT, REVERSE THE JUDGMENT OF DISMISSAL OF PETITIONER'S CLAIMS FOR MALICIOUS PROSECUTION, AND REMAND THE CASE TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID TWO-THIRDS BY RESPONDENTS AND ONE-THIRD BY PETITIONER. Dissenting Opinion follows:.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) affirmance of trial court's dismissal for untimely notice under Maryland Local Government Tort Claims Act, Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 5-301 (1987, 1998 Repl. Vol., 2000 Supp.), of actions against respondent officials for malicious prosecution, false arrest, and false imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: After petitioner's acquittals of disorderly conduct and related offenses, he sued respondent police and officials for malicious prosecution, false arrest, and false imprisonment. The Maryland Local Government Tort Claims Act, Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 5-301 (1987, 1998 Repl. Vol., 2000 Supp.), required that claimants give notice of claims within 180 days of injury. The court held that petitioner's failure to give notice until soon after his acquittal made his false arrest and imprisonment claims untimely, since these became legal injuries as soon as he was released from custody. However, the malicious prosecution claim did not accrue until petitioner was acquitted, so petitioner's notice was timely. Petitioner argued that untimely notice should have been waived for good cause, because he had been preoccupied with his criminal defense. After looking to other jurisdictions' decisions, the court held that, since giving of notice was not a complicated task, petitioner failed to show good cause for delay.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed dismissal of false arrest and false imprisonment claims, because they accrued when petitioner was released, but reversed and remanded for further proceedings on malicious prosecution claim, because it did not accrue until petitioner's acquittal. Preparation of criminal defense was insufficient grounds for waiver of time limits.

DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR TALBOT COUNTY REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THE CIRCUIT COURT WITH DIRECTIONS TO AFFIRM THE DECISION OF THE BOARD OF APPEALS OF TALBOT COUNTY IN BOARD APPEAL NO. 1053; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLEE.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Court issued writ of certiorari to appellants on its own initiative, from Circuit Court for Talbot County (Maryland) judgment for appellees, to consider whether Title II of Americans with Disabilities Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 12132, applied to administration and enforcement of county zoning ordinance governing variances for property within specified critical shoreline area.

The court therefore reversed on different grounds, reinstating the board decision, holding the board rightly recognized the need to reasonably accommodate disabled persons despite and within zoning restrictions and applied the appropriate criteria; substantial evidence supported its decision.


OUTCOME: Judgment was reversed on different ground and case was remanded with directions to affirm county board of appeals' decision for appellants, as there was no basis to reverse board decision, given substantial evidence before board and its application of appropriate variance criteria to that evidence, both accommodating disabled homeowner's needs and satisfying requirements of zoning ordinance.

Post-conviction proceeding at State v. Ware, 373 Md. 550, 819 A.2d 1030, 2002 Md. LEXIS 1092 (2002)
